Divine Attributes
intermediate
8 min
Attributes of Divine Will: Eternal, General, Without Purpose
Sanusi demonstrates that divine will is eternal, general, without purpose, and that rulings have no cause.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand that God's will is eternal, general over all possible things, and not for any purpose, and that divine rulings have no rational cause.

Core Idea
God's will has no cause and no purpose; it is eternal and universal.

Sanusi now turns to the attribute of divine will. He establishes three key points: it is eternal, it encompasses all possible things without exception, and it is not motivated by any purpose. He then extends this principle to divine rulings, arguing they have no rational cause and are known only through revelation. This lesson unpacks these foundational aspects of divine will.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without analysis, then read the literal translation. Get the overall flow of Sanusi's argument.
Notice how the passage moves from will to rulings.
2
Identify the three attributes of will
Find where Sanusi states that will must be eternal, general, and not for a purpose. Note the reasoning for each.
The first line summarizes all three.
3
Understand the analogy with power
Sanusi uses the earlier discussion of power to argue for will. Compare the two: both must be eternal to avoid regress; both must be general to avoid origination.
The analogy is central to the proof.
4
Apply the principle to rulings
See how Sanusi extends the idea to divine judgments: they have no cause and are known only by revelation. Any causal language in scripture is interpretative.
This shows the consistency of Sanusi's theology.
5
Reflect on the conclusion
Re-read the summary statement: 'His acts and judgments have no cause'. Consider how this shapes Islamic theology.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
عموم الإرادة للممكنات ، وتنزيه الإرادة القديمة عن أن تكون لغرض ] ويلزمُ أنْ يكونَ ذلك بإرادة قديمة عامة في جميعِ الممكناتِ ، خيراً كانَتْ أو شرّاً؛ لما عرفت قبل في القدرة، وأن تكونَ إرادته لا لغرض له ، وإلا لكان ناقصاً في ذاتِهِ مُتكملاً بفعلِهِ ، وذلك محال ، ولا لغرض لخلْقِهِ وإلا وجب عليهِ مراعاة الصلاح والأصلح لهم ، وهو لما سيأتي (١) محال وكما استحالَ أنْ يريد سبحانَهُ أو يفعل لغرض .. كذلكَ استحالَ أنْ يكونَ حكمه على فعل بوجوب أو تحريم أو غيرهما مِنَ الأحكام الشرعيَّةِ لغرضِ مِنَ الأغراض ؛ لأنَّ الأفعال كلَّها مستويةً في أنَّها خلقه واختراعه ، فتعين بعضها للإيجاب وبعضها للتحريم أو غيره .. واقع بمحض الاختيار ، لا سبب له ولا مجال للعقل فيه أصلاً ، وإنَّما يُعرَفُ بالشرع فقط وبالجملة : فأفعالُهُ تعالى وأحكامه لا علة لها ، وما يُوجَدُ مِنَ التعليلِ لذلكَ في كلام أهل الشرع فمُؤوّل بالأمارات أو نحوها مما يصح . الإشارة بـ ( ذلكَ ( ترجع إلى كونه مريداً ؛ أي : يلزم أن تكونَ مريديَّتُه تعالى بإرادة ، وأن تكونَ تلكَ الإرادة قديمة ، وأن تكونَ عامةً الممكناتِ ، خيراً كانَتْ أو شرّاً ، طاعة كانَتْ أو معصية في جميع وقوله : ( لما عرفت قبلُ في القدرةِ ) يعني : فكما أَنَّهُ لا يُعقَلُ قادرٌ لا قدرة له . . كذلكَ لا يُعقَلُ مريدٌ لا إرادة له ، وكما أنَّ القدرة يجب قدمها وإلا لكانَ ضدُّها - وهو العجزُ - قديماً ، والقديم لا ينعدم أبداً ، فيلزمُ ألا تُوجَدَ القدرة أبداً ، فلا يُوجَدَ شيءٌ مِنَ العالم أبداً ؛ لتوقُفِ وجوده على القدرة . وأيضاً : فلو كانَتِ القدرة حادثةً لاحتاجَتْ في حدوثها إلى قدرة أخرى ، ويلزم التسلسل ، كذلكَ يُقالُ في الإرادة : لو كانت حادثة لاحتاجت إلى إرادة أخرى ، ولزم التسلسل وكما وجب عمومُ القدرة لجميع الممكنات وإلا لزم حدوثها
Generality of will for possible things, and the transcendence of the eternal will from being for a purpose. And it is necessary that this be by an eternal will, general in all possible things, whether good or evil, because of what you knew before concerning power, and that His will is not for a purpose, otherwise He would be deficient in His essence, perfecting Himself by His act, and that is impossible, nor for a purpose for His creation, otherwise He would be obliged to observe what is beneficial and most beneficial for them, and that is impossible as will come. And just as it is impossible that He, glorified be He, should will or act for a purpose, similarly it is impossible that His judgment on an act of obligation or prohibition or other legal rulings should be for any purpose; because all acts are equal in that they are His creation and invention, so specifying some for obligation and others for prohibition or other is done by pure choice, having no cause and with no scope for the intellect in it at all; it is only known by revelation alone. In summary: His acts and judgments have no cause, and what is found of causation for that in the speech of the people of revelation is interpreted as indicators or the like that are valid. The reference by 'that' goes back to His being willing; meaning: it is necessary that His willed objects be by a will, and that that will be eternal, and that it be general in all possible things, whether good or evil, whether obedience or disobedience. And his statement: 'because of what you knew before concerning power' means: just as a powerful being without power is inconceivable, similarly a willing being without will is inconceivable. And just as power must be eternal, otherwise its opposite—which is inability—would be eternal, and the eternal never ceases to exist, so power would never exist, and thus nothing of the world would ever exist, since its existence depends on power. Also, if power were originated, it would need another power for its origination, leading to infinite regress. The same is said concerning will: if it were originated, it would need another will, leading to infinite regress. And just as the generality of power for all possible things is necessary, otherwise it would entail its origination.
Source pages p. 284

Commentary
This passage is a proof and explanation of the attributes of divine will. It begins by stating the necessity of an eternal will that is general for all possible things, good and evil, and that it is not for any purpose. The argument is analogous to the earlier discussion on divine power. Then it extends the same reasoning to divine rulings, asserting that they have no cause and are known only through revelation, and that any causal language in scripture is interpreted figuratively. The author then explains the reference: the will itself must be eternal and general. The passage uses an analogy with power to argue against temporal origination: if will were originated, it would need another will, leading to infinite regress. It also argues that generality is necessary, otherwise the will would be originated. Throughout, the author follows the order of first establishing the attributes of will (eternal, general, without purpose), then supporting them with the analogy of power, and finally concluding that divine rulings likewise have no cause.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think that God's will is for a purpose or that His rulings have rational causes.
Correction: The passage clarifies that divine will and judgments are without cause and not for any purpose; they are known only through revelation.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what would follow if God's will were originated (haditha)?
A) It would be eternal.
B) It would not need any cause.
C) It would need another will, leading to infinite regress.
D) It would be general only for good things.

Key Takeaway
God's will is eternal, universal, and without purpose; likewise, His rulings have no cause and are known only through revelation.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you face a trial or evil in the world, do you genuinely submit to the fact that God's will has no purpose you can comprehend, or do you seek a rational explanation to comfort yourself? Are you willing to accept that His will is beyond your understanding?
?
The passage says God's judgments have no cause and are known only by revelation. Do you ever find yourself trying to justify Islamic rulings by their apparent benefits (e.g., health, social order) rather than simply obeying because Allah commanded? How does this passage challenge your reliance on rational justification for your faith?
?
If God's will is not for any purpose and His rulings have no cause, does your worship become purely submission, or do you secretly expect reward and benefits? Can you worship Allah without any expectation of return, simply because He is worthy?

Divine Attributes

Proof That Divine Will Is Eternal and General
Sanusi proves divine will's eternity and generality by analogy with power.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why divine will must be both eternal and all-encompassing, as proven by strict analogy with divine power.

Core Idea
God's will mirrors His power: both must be eternal and universal.

Sanusi establishes the eternity and universality of divine will by drawing a rigorous analogy with divine power. Just as power cannot be temporal or limited, will cannot be either. In this passage, he walks through the consequences of supposing will is temporal or restricted.

Guided Path
1
Recognize the three claims
The passage opens by stating that God's attribute of willing (irāda) must exist, be eternal, and be general over all possible things.
Look for the words 'قديمة' and 'عامة' in the Arabic.
2
Understand the analogy with power
The proof rests on symmetry between will and power. If a temporal power leads to absurdities, so does a temporal will.
Notice the phrase 'كما عرفت قبلُ في القدرة' (as you knew before concerning power).
3
Examine the argument for eternity
If will were temporal, its opposite (non-will) would be eternal, preventing will's existence, and it would require another will to bring it about (infinite regress).
The two sub-arguments: (1) opposite eternal → never exists, (2) need for another will → regress.
4
Examine the argument for generality
If will were not general, it would need a specifier (mukhassis), making it temporal or turning a possible into an impossibility.
The same logic as for power: lack of generality implies need for a specifier, which implies temporality.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الإشارة بـ ( ذلكَ ( ترجع إلى كونه مريداً ؛ أي : يلزم أن تكونَ مريديَّتُه تعالى بإرادة ، وأن تكونَ تلكَ الإرادة قديمة ، وأن تكونَ عامةً الممكناتِ ، خيراً كانَتْ أو شرّاً ، طاعة كانَتْ أو معصية في جميع وقوله : ( لما عرفت قبلُ في القدرةِ ) يعني : فكما أَنَّهُ لا يُعقَلُ قادرٌ لا قدرة له . . كذلكَ لا يُعقَلُ مريدٌ لا إرادة له ، وكما أنَّ القدرة يجب قدمها وإلا لكانَ ضدُّها - وهو العجزُ - قديماً ، والقديم لا ينعدم أبداً ، فيلزمُ ألا تُوجَدَ القدرة أبداً ، فلا يُوجَدَ شيءٌ مِنَ العالم أبداً ؛ لتوقُفِ وجوده على القدرة . وأيضاً : فلو كانَتِ القدرة حادثةً لاحتاجَتْ في حدوثها إلى قدرة أخرى ، ويلزم التسلسل ، كذلكَ يُقالُ في الإرادة : لو كانت حادثة لاحتاجت إلى إرادة أخرى ، ولزم التسلسل وكما وجب عمومُ القدرة لجميع الممكنات وإلا لزم حدوثها ؛ للافتقار إلى المخصّص ، أو لزم انقلابُ الممكن مستحيلاً . . كذلك يلزم حرفاً بحرفٍ في الإرادة
The indication by 'that' (dhālika) refers to His being willing; i.e., it is necessary that His being willing be by a will, and that that will be eternal, and that it be general over all possible things, whether good or evil, obedience or sin, in all. And His saying: 'as you knew before concerning power (al-qudra)' means: just as it is not intelligible that there be a powerful being without power, likewise it is not intelligible that there be a willing being without will. And just as power must be eternal, otherwise its opposite—which is incapacity—would be eternal, and the eternal never ceases to exist, so it would follow that power would never exist, and thus nothing of the world would ever exist, because its existence depends on power. Also: if power were temporal, it would need another power for its temporal origination, leading to infinite regress. The same is said concerning will: if it were temporal, it would need another will, and infinite regress would follow. And just as the generality of power over all possible things is necessary, otherwise its temporality would follow due to need for a specificr, or a possible thing would become impossible, likewise the same applies letter for letter to will.
Source pages p. 285

Commentary
This passage provides a detailed proof that divine will (irāda) must be eternal and general over all possible things, by strict analogy with divine power (qudra). The author first sets up the analogy: just as a powerful being requires power, a willing being requires will. Then he argues for eternity: if power were temporal, its opposite (incapacity) would be eternal, but the eternal never ceases, so power would never come to exist, making the world impossible. Also, a temporal power would require another power to bring it about, leading to infinite regress. The same arguments apply to will. Finally, he argues for generality: if power were not general over all possibles, it would need a specificr to determine its objects, implying temporality, or the possible would become impossible. The same holds for will. This establishes that God's will is eternal and encompasses all things, including good and evil, obedience and sin.

Key Terms
إرادة
قديمة
عامة
قدرة
تسلسل
مخصّص

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: If God wills evil, then God is evil.
Correction: The proof shows that divine will encompasses all possible things including evil and sin, but this does not attribute moral evil to God; it only concerns the attribute of will itself.

Checkpoint
What is one of the arguments used to prove that divine will must be eternal?
A) If will were temporal, its opposite would be eternal and prevent its existence
B) If will were temporal, it would need a creator
C) If will were eternal, it would be uncreated
D) If will were general, it would include impossibilities

Key Takeaway
Divine will must be eternal and all-encompassing, exactly as divine power is, because the same logical consequences follow from supposing otherwise.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
If God's will encompasses all things, including your sins, how does that change the way you view your own failures? Are you quick to blame circumstance or to take responsibility, knowing that every action is within God's will? What will you do in your next moment of sin to remember this?
?
This lesson shows that God's will is eternal and all-encompassing, just as His power is. How does this truth confront your desire to control your own life? In what area are you resisting surrender to God's will?
?
The proof argues that if will were temporal, it would require another will, leading to infinite regress. This shows God's will is ultimate and not dependent. How does this settle your heart when you feel uncertain about the future? What specific fear can you entrust to God's eternal will today?

On Attributing God's Will to Disbelief and Sin
Sanusi discusses the permissibility of saying 'God wills' specifically for disbelief and sin.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why some scholars forbid saying 'God wills disbelief' specifically, and how to properly express God's will while maintaining adab.

Core Idea
Politeness in speech about God's will does not deny His all-encompassing will.

This lesson examines a subtle point in Islamic theology: the permissibility of attributing God's will specifically to acts like disbelief and sin. While all things occur by God's will according to Ahl al-Sunnah, scholars differed on how to express this without implying that God is described by such acts. The following passage by Sanusi presents the reasoning, the proper expression, and Quranic evidence for maintaining adab in speech about God.

Guided Path
1
Consensus on God's universal will
Read the first part where Sanusi states the consensus that all existent things—faith, disbelief, obedience, sin—occur only by God's will. Note the inclusive scope.
This is the foundational premise.
2
Disagreement on specific attribution
Identify the disagreement: some scholars forbid saying 'God wills disbelief' or 'God wills sin' specifically, on grounds of adab.
The prohibition is not about belief but about expression.
3
Reasoning: avoiding misconception
Understand the reasoning: to prevent the misconception that God is described by disbelief or sin. God creates the action but the servant acquires it and is described by it.
The analogy of the container helps clarify.
4
Proper expression: generalization
Learn the recommended expression: generalize all things with 'God wills' to include disbelief and sin implicitly, preserving adab. Specification is allowed only for good acts when no misunderstanding arises.
5
Quranic evidence for adab
Examine the Quranic examples: in al-Fatiha, God attributes favor to Himself but does not directly attribute anger; in al-Jinn, evil is mentioned without direct attribution.
These show the principle of politeness in divine speech.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ثم مع إجماع أهل السنة على أن الكائنات كلها إنما تقع بإرادة الله تعالى، ولا فرق في ذلك بين الإيمان والكفر، وبين الطاعات والمعاصي، وغير ذلك من سائر الممكنات. اختلفوا في إطلاق لفظ إرادته تعالى لخصوص الكفر والمعصية مثلاً: فمنهم من منع على طريق الأدب فقط؛ لدفع توهم أن الفاعل يستحق اسم الكفر والمعصية باعتبار إضافته إلى الله تعالى، وليس كذلك، وإنما ذلك الاسم للفعل المخلوق لله تعالى المراد له باعتبار وجوده في ذات العبد وإضافته إليه، فالعبد هو الموصوف بالكفر والمعصية وإن لم يكن مخترعاً لهما، ومولانا جل وعلا لا يتصف بهما وإن كان هو المخترع لهما، وهكذا سائر الأفعال؛ إنما يوصف تعالى بأنه مخترع لها مريد لها، لا أنه يوصف بشيء منها؛ لاستحالة انصاف ذاته العلية بالحوادث. وتقريبه في الشاهد: أنك لو وضعت شيئاً في إناء، ولذلك الشيء رائحة قبيحة أو لون قبيح لكان المكتسب لذلك القبح أو المتصف به وإن لم يكن له أثر فيه ألبتة ذلك الإناء، لا أنت الذي وضعت ذلك الشيء فيه. وبالجملة: فالأفعال كلها بالنسبة إليه تعالى حسنة، وإنما افترقت باعتبار وجودها في العباد بحسب ما اكتسبوا منها شرعاً أو عرفاً، وإن لم يكن لهم أثر في شيء منها ألبتة. ووجه أيضاً هذا القول: بأن تخصيص الكفر والمعصية بإسنادهما إلى إرادة الله تعالى دون غيرهما يصير شبه الاعتذار بذلك في دفع الدم اللاحق للكافر والعاصي شرعاً، وذلك ليس بعذر في الشرع، ولا يسأل تعالى عما يفعل أو يحكم. وكيفية التعبير على هذا القول: أن يعمم جميع الكائنات بلفظ الإرادة، فيفهم من التعميم دخول الكفر والمعاصي مع المحافظة على حسن الأدب في التعبير، وله أن يخصص على هذا القول إطلاق لفظ الإرادة على الطاعات، وما يعد من المحاسن شرعاً أو عرفاً؛ لسلامة العبارة إذ ذاك من سوء الأدب، وينبغي أن يخصص هذا بما إذا لم يكن في السامعين من يفهم من هذا التخصيص أن المعاصي ليست مرادة له تعالى، أما إذا كانت فيتعين التعميم لا غير. ومما يشهد لهذا القول في طلب مراعاة الأدب: قوله: ﴿ صِرَاطَ الَّذِينَ أَنْعَمْتَ عَلَيْهِمْ [الفاتحة: 7] فأسند ذلك لنفسه العليَّة، ثم قال: غَيْرِ الْمَغْضُوبِ عَلَيْهِمْ، ولم يقل: (غير الذين غضبت عليهم). وقوله: ﴿ وَأَنَّا لَا نَدْرِى أَشَرٌّ أُرِيدَ بِمَن فِي الْأَرْضِ ﴾ [الجن: ١٠] فأسند فعل
Then, with the consensus of Ahl al-Sunnah that all existent things occur only by the will of God—and there is no difference in that between faith and disbelief, or between acts of obedience and acts of disobedience, or any other of the possible things—they disagreed about the application of the word 'His will' specifically to disbelief and sin, for example. Among them are those who forbade it only as a matter of politeness (adab), to repel the misconception that the agent [i.e., God] deserves the name of disbelief and sin by virtue of being attributed to God—but that is not the case. Rather, that name belongs to the action created by God and willed by Him, by virtue of its existence in the servant and its attribution to him. So the servant is described by disbelief and sin even if he did not originate them, and our Lord, glorified and exalted, is not described by them even though He is their originator. And likewise all actions: God is described only as the originator of them and the willer of them, not that He is described by any of them, because it is impossible for His high essence to be qualified by accidents. Its analogy in the witnessed [world] is that if you placed something in a container, and that thing had a foul smell or ugly color, then the container acquires that ugliness or is described by it even though it has no effect in it at all—that container, not you who placed that thing in it. In summary: all actions are good relative to Him, and they only differ by virtue of their existence in servants according to what they have acquired from them by sacred law or custom, even though they have no effect in any of them at all. This opinion was also justified by saying that specifying disbelief and sin by attributing them to the will of God to the exclusion of others becomes akin to excusing them in repelling the legal punishment that becomes due to the disbeliever and sinner, and that is not an excuse in the Law, and God is not asked about what He does or judges. The manner of expression according to this opinion is that one should generalize all existent things with the word 'will', so that it is understood from the generalization that disbelief and sins are included, while preserving good politeness in expression. One may, according to this opinion, specify the application of the word 'will' to acts of obedience and what is counted as good by law or custom, because the expression is then safe from bad politeness, but this specification should be made only when there are no listeners who understand from this specification that sins are not willed by Him. If there are, then generalization is necessarily the only option. Among what bears witness to this opinion in requiring observance of politeness is His saying: 'The path of those upon whom You have bestowed favor' (al-Fatiha 7)—He attributed that to His noble self, then said: 'Not of those who have incurred Your anger', and did not say: 'not of those with whom You are angry'. And His saying: 'And we do not know whether evil is intended for those on earth' (al-Jinn 10)—so He attributed the action...
Source pages p. 286

Commentary
This passage discusses a nuanced theological and linguistic issue: whether it is permissible to say 'God wills disbelief' or 'God wills sin' specifically, given that all things occur by God's will according to Ahl al-Sunnah. The author first states the consensus that all existent things happen by God's will without distinction. Then he presents a disagreement about the wording: some scholars forbid the specific application of the word 'will' to acts like disbelief and sin, on the grounds of politeness (adab). The reason is to avoid the misconception that God Himself is described as a disbeliever or sinner. In reality, the action is created by God but attributed to the servant who acquires it; the servant is the one described by the action's moral quality. God is only the creator and willer, not the subject of the moral description. The author provides an analogy: if you place a foul-smelling object in a container, the container acquires the smell, not you. Similarly, God's actions are all good relative to Him; they only appear as good or bad due to the servant's acquisition and the legal or customary framework. The author then mentions a further justification: if one specifically attributes disbelief and sin to God's will, it might appear as an excuse to avoid punishment, but that is not valid in Sharia. The proper expression is to generalize: say 'God wills all things,' which implicitly includes disbelief and sins while maintaining politeness. One may specify the will for good acts only when no one misunderstands that sins are not willed. Finally, the author cites Quranic evidence: in Surah al-Fatiha, God attributes favor to Himself but does not attribute anger directly; similarly in Surah al-Jinn, the evil is attributed without naming the agent directly. These show the principle of politeness in speech about God.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Forbidding the phrase 'God wills disbelief' implies that disbelief occurs without God's will.
Correction: The prohibition is only a matter of adab; disbelief still occurs by God's will, but one should avoid wording that could imply God is described by disbelief.

Checkpoint
Why do some scholars forbid saying 'God wills disbelief' specifically, according to this passage?
A) They deny that God wills disbelief at all.
B) It is out of politeness to avoid the misconception that God is described by disbelief.
C) They consider it a sin to attribute any action to God.
D) They fear that disbelief would not be punished.

Key Takeaway
It is adab to avoid specifically attributing disbelief and sin to God's will, even though all things are by His will, to prevent the misconception that God is described by such acts.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you speak about God's will in your daily life, do you ever hesitate to attribute difficult events to Him? How does this lesson challenge you to be both truthful and polite in your speech?
?
Reflect on a sin you struggle with. Do you sometimes feel that since God willed it, you are not responsible? How does Sanusi's explanation of 'acquisition' call you to take ownership of your actions without excusing them?
?
If you heard someone say 'God willed that person's disbelief,' how would you respond after studying this passage? What would you say to preserve both theological truth and adab?

Proof That God's Will Is Not for Any Purpose
Sanusi proves that God's will is not for any purpose by showing both possible purposes are impossible.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the rational proof that God's will is not motivated by any purpose, and the two arguments against the possibility of God acting for His own benefit.

Core Idea
God's actions are not for any purpose—He is absolutely self-sufficient.

In this passage, Sanusi presents a concise proof that God's will is not driven by any external purpose or benefit. By dividing the possible purposes into those returning to God and those returning to creation, he shows that the first option is rationally impossible. This proof reinforces the Islamic doctrine of God's absolute transcendence and self-sufficiency.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and its translation
First, read the Arabic passage carefully, then the literal translation. Try to grasp the overall structure of the proof.
Notice that the passage begins with a definition and then presents a proof.
2
Identify the two possible purposes
The proof divides the hypothetical purpose into two categories: a benefit returning to God or a benefit returning to creation. Identify these in the text.
3
Understand the two reasons against benefit to God
The first possibility (benefit to God) is rejected for two reasons: it would entail accidents on God's essence, and it would imply God was deficient before the act. Understand each reason and why it is invalid.
The second possibility (benefit to creation) is not refuted here, but it is assumed to be possible without implying deficiency.
4
Reflect on the theological significance
Consider why this proof is important: it affirms that God is self-sufficient and His actions are free, not compelled by any need or purpose.
This connects to the broader Islamic understanding of divine transcendence (tanzih).

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله : ( وأنْ تكونَ إرادته لا لغرض له ) يعني : أنَّه يستحيلُ أنْ تكونَ إرادته تعالى لإيجادِ فعلِ مِنَ الأفعال أو إعدامه لغرض مِنَ الأغراض؛ أي: لا علةَ لشيءٍ مِنَ الأفعال بحيث تكون العلة تبعثه تعالى على إيجاد فعل أو إعدامه، بل هو جلَّ وعلا مختار في كلا الأمرين. واستدل على هذا المطلب في العقيدة: بأن الغرض الذي يُقدَّرُ أنَّ الفعل كانَ لأجله؛ إما أن يكون مصلحة تعود إليه تعالى، أو مصلحةً تعود لخلقه. والأوَّلُ باطل لوجهين: أحدهما: استلزامُهُ أنْ تكونَ ذاتُهُ العليَّةُ تتجدَّدُ عليها الحوادث وهو باطل؛ لما سيأتي في فضل الحياة. الثاني: استلزامهُ أنْ يكونَ تعالى جلَّ وعزَّ ناقصاً في ذاتِهِ العليَّةِ الغنيَّةِ، ويتكمَّلَ بأفعالِهِ؛ لأنَّ كمالَ تلكَ المصلحة قد فاتته على هذا الفَرْضِ قبلَ خلْقِ الفعل الذي وُجِدَتْ معَهُ، وفوتُ الكمال نقص وهو تعالى مُنزَّه عنه بإجماع العقلاء.
His statement: 'And that His will is not for a purpose for Him' means that it is impossible for His will, may He be exalted, to be for the purpose of bringing a certain act into existence or annihilating it for any purpose; that is, there is no cause for any act such that the cause impels Him, Most High, to bring an act into existence or annihilate it, but He, Glorious and Most High, is free in both matters. And he argued for this matter in al-‘Aqida: that the purpose for which the act is supposed to have been done is either a benefit returning to Him, Most High, or a benefit returning to His creation. The first is invalid for two reasons: one of them is that it would entail that His Lofty Essence would have accidents occurring upon it, and that is invalid, as will come in the section on life. The second is that it would entail that He, Glorious and Most High, is deficient in His Lofty, Self-Sufficient Essence and becomes perfect through His acts; because the perfection of that benefit would have escaped Him on this assumption before the creation of the act with which it came into being, and the loss of perfection is a deficiency, and He, Most High, is transcendent above it by consensus of the rational people.
Source pages p. 289

Commentary
This passage is a proof (dalil) for the doctrine that God's will is not for any purpose. The author first explains the statement: it means that God's will to create or annihilate any act is not caused by any purpose; there is no external cause that impels Him. He acts as a free agent. The proof then proceeds by dividing the hypothetical purpose: it would either be a benefit to God Himself or to His creation. The first option (benefit to God) is impossible for two reasons. First, if God's act were for His own benefit, then His essence would be subject to accidents (hawadith) – that is, new events would occur in His essence – which is impossible because God is eternal and changeless. Second, if God acted for His own benefit, it would imply that before the act, He lacked that benefit, meaning He was deficient, and He would become perfect through His act. But God is absolutely perfect and free from any deficiency, as agreed by all rational people. Thus, the first option is invalid. The second option (benefit to creation) is not explicitly refuted here, but it is assumed that it is possible and does not imply deficiency in God. The proof establishes that God's actions are not motivated by any need or purpose.

Key Terms
إرادة
غرض
علة
مصلحة
استلزام
الحوادث
النقص
إجماع العقلاء

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Denying purpose in God's actions makes them arbitrary or meaningless.
Correction: Denying purpose affirms God's absolute self-sufficiency and freedom; His actions are not driven by need but by His own wisdom and will.

Checkpoint
What is the first reason given against the idea that God acts for His own benefit?
A) It would imply that God is imperfect.
B) It would imply that new events occur in God's essence.
C) It would imply that God is compelled.
D) It would imply that God is not all-powerful.

Key Takeaway
God's will is not for any purpose because acting for one's own benefit would imply imperfection or change, both impossible for God.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you perform acts of worship, do you ever feel that you are giving God something He needs? How does knowing that God is absolutely self-sufficient and does not act for any purpose change the way you approach your worship? Will you recommit to worshiping Him solely out of love and obedience, not out of a sense that He is lacking without it?
?
If God's commands are not for His own benefit but for yours, what does that tell you about His mercy and wisdom? When you disobey, are you aware that you are harming only yourself, not God? How should this realization affect your repentance and your effort to follow His commands?

No Purpose for Allah's Will: First Kind Refuted
Sanusi divides the purpose and refutes the first kind with two arguments.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Sanusi's proof that Allah's will cannot have a purpose, specifically why a purpose benefiting Allah is impossible.

Core Idea
A purpose that benefits Allah would imply deficiency, which is impossible.

Sanusi begins by stating that Allah's will is not for any purpose. He then divides the supposed purpose into two categories. In this lesson, we examine the first category: a purpose that would benefit Allah Himself. Sanusi gives two powerful arguments to show why this is impossible, both based on the nature of divine perfection.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once, then the translation. Note that Sanusi first defines the claim and then presents the division of purposes.
Pay special attention to the two reasons given for rejecting the first kind.
2
Identify the two reasons
The passage states two reasons why a purpose benefiting Allah is false. Find each one and understand how they both lead to an impossibility.
3
Reflect on the implications
Ask yourself: What would it mean if Allah needed a purpose? How do these reasons protect the concept of divine perfection?
This connects to the core idea of Allah's self-sufficiency.
4
Relate to broader kalam
Notice that the first reason references future discussions (e.g., tajaddud al-hawadith). This shows how arguments connect across the text.

Source Text

وهو أنَّ المصلحة التي قُدِّرَ أَنَّ الفعلَ خُلِقَ لأجلِها إِنَّما تعودُ إلى المخلوقِ ، لا إليهِ تعالى - فهو باطل أيضاً ؛ لأنَّهُ لو كان تعالى يبعثه على الفعل إيصال المصالح للعباد.. لكـانَ مراعاة الصلاح والأصلح لهم واجباً عليه عقلاً كما تقوله المعتزلة ، وهو ظاهرُ البطلان ، وسيأتي دليلُ بطلانِهِ في باب ما يجوز في حقه تعالى(۱) وأقرب شيء يدلك على بطلانه : إيلام الله تعالى للأطفال والبهائم ، ولا صلاحَ لهم في ذلك قطعاً ، وإِنْ قُدِّرَ ثَمَّ مصلحةٌ فهو قادر أن يوصلها بغير إيلام ، وكذا تخليد عذابِ الكافرِ معَ مساواتِهِ للمؤمن المخلَّدِ في النعيم مع أنَّ كلاً منهما لا أثرَ لهُ في شيءٍ مِنْ أفعاله ، وكذا تكليفُ الخلْقِ في الدنيا ؛ أي : لا مصلحة لهم فيهِ فإنْ قالوا : حصول عظيمِ الثواب لهم على تكليف المشاق . قلنا : لا أثر لهم في شيءٍ مِنْ تلك الأفعالِ ، فاستوى مَنْ فعلَ ومَنْ لم يفعل وأيضاً : فمولانا جل وعلا قادر على إيصال ذلكَ الثواب العظيمِ لهم بغير تكليف ولا فعل لهم أصلاً ومن أدلَّةِ إِبطالِ تعليل أفعالِ اللهِ تعالى وإرادته بالأغراض : أنَّ الغرضَ إِمَّا أنْ يكونَ قديماً ، فيلزم قدمُ الفعل ، وإلا كانَ الباري جلَّ وعلا ناقصاً ؛ لفواتِ غرضِهِ ، أو حادثاً ، فيحتاج هذا الغرضُ في
His statement: 'And that His will is not for a purpose' means: it is impossible for His will – exalted is He – to be for the purpose of bringing any act into existence or annihilating it for any purpose; i.e., there is no cause for any act such that the cause impels Him – exalted is He – to bring an act into existence or annihilate it. Rather, He – majestic and exalted – is free to choose in both matters. [Proof of the falsity of His acts being for a purpose] He argued for this point in the Creed: that the purpose for which the act is supposed to have been done is either a benefit returning to Him – exalted is He – or a benefit returning to His creation. The first is false for two reasons: One: it entails that His high essence undergoes new events, and this is false as will come in the section on life (1). Two: it entails that He – majestic and exalted – is deficient in His high, self-sufficient essence and becomes perfect through His acts, because the perfection of that benefit would have been missed by Him on this assumption before the creation of the act with which it came, and missing perfection is deficiency, and He – exalted is He – is free of that by consensus of the rational people. And [?], or the bag and the incapacity, for it contains that the acquisitions of servants are also by decree. 289 (1) It will come (p. 331). As for the second division – that the benefit for which the act is supposed to have been created only returns to the creature, not to Him – exalted is He – it is also false; because if He – exalted is He – were impelled to the act by conveying benefits to servants, then observing what is beneficial and most beneficial for them would be intellectually obligatory upon Him, as the Mu'tazila say, and that is clearly false; and its proof of falsity will come in the chapter on what is possible concerning Him – exalted is He (1). And the closest thing indicating its falsity is Allah's inflicting pain on children and animals, and there is absolutely no benefit for them in that; and if a benefit is supposed, He is able to convey it without inflicting pain. Similarly, the eternal punishment of the disbeliever despite his similarity to the believer who is eternally in bliss, while each of them has no effect on anything of His acts. Similarly, the obligation of creation in this world; i.e., there is no benefit for them in it. If they say: the attainment of great reward for them is due to the obligation of hardships. We say: they have no effect in any of those acts, so the one who does and the one who does not are equal. Also: our Master – majestic and exalted – is able to convey that great reward to them without obligation or any act on their part. Among the proofs of invalidating the attribution of purposes to Allah's acts and His will: that the purpose is either eternal, then the eternity of the act follows, otherwise the Creator – majestic and exalted – would be deficient due to the loss of His purpose; or temporal, then this purpose needs...
Source pages p. 289

Commentary
This passage contains a proof from Sanusi that Allah's will and acts cannot be for any purpose (gharad). He begins by stating the claim: it is impossible for Allah's will to be for any purpose, meaning there is no cause (illa) impelling Him to act. Then he provides the proof. He divides the supposed purpose into two kinds: (1) a benefit returning to Allah Himself, and (2) a benefit returning to His creation. He refutes both. For the first kind, he gives two arguments: (a) it would entail that Allah's essence undergoes new events (tajaddud al-hawadith), which is false; (b) it would entail that Allah is deficient and becomes perfect through His acts, because the benefit would have been missed before the act, implying deficiency – which is impossible by consensus. For the second kind (benefit to creation), he argues that if Allah were impelled by conveying benefits, then observing what is beneficial (al-salah) and most beneficial (al-aslah) would be obligatory upon Him, as the Mu'tazila hold – but that is false. He gives concrete examples: Allah inflicts pain on children and animals with no benefit, and if any benefit were supposed, Allah could convey it without pain. Similarly, eternal punishment of disbelievers and eternal bliss of believers are not based on any effect of their actions. Also, obligation (taklif) itself yields no benefit; if the reward is the purpose, Allah could grant it without any obligation. Finally, he adds another proof: the purpose must be either eternal (qadim) or temporal (hadith). If eternal, the act would also be eternal (impossible for temporal acts). If temporal, the purpose itself would need a cause, leading to infinite regress. The text cuts off mid-sentence at the page end, but the argument is clear.

إرادة
غرض
مصلحة
علة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think Allah acts for a purpose like humans, seeking benefit for Himself.
Correction: Sanusi shows this would imply deficiency or change in Allah, which is impossible.

Checkpoint

A) One
B) Two
C) Three
D) Four

Key Takeaway
Allah's will cannot have a purpose that benefits Him, because that would imply deficiency or change.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

When you face trials, do you ever wonder why Allah allows them? This lesson teaches that His will has no purpose that benefits Him. How does this free you from trying to decipher a hidden 'reason' behind His decrees, and instead submit to His absolute wisdom?
?
The argument states that a purpose would imply deficiency in Allah. Examine your own du'a (supplication): do you sometimes feel that Allah 'needs' your worship to complete His plan? Repent from that subtle anthropomorphism, and commit to worshipping Him as the Perfect, Self-Sufficient One.

No Purpose for Allah's Will: Second Kind Refuted
Sanusi refutes the second kind of purpose with examples and logical arguments.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Sanusi's refutation of the claim that Allah's acts have a purpose benefiting creation.

Core Idea
If Allah acted for creation's benefit, then observing the most beneficial would be obligatory—which is false.

After refuting the first kind of purpose, Sanusi turns to the second: that Allah's acts are for the benefit of creation. He argues that this would make certain things obligatory upon Allah, which is false. He provides concrete examples like the suffering of children and animals, eternal punishment, and the obligation of religious duties to show that no such purpose exists.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage
Read the Arabic and translation. Note the initial conditional statement: if Allah were impelled by benefit to creation, then observing the most beneficial would be obligatory.
2
Examine the examples
Sanusi gives three examples: pain of children/animals, eternal punishment vs. bliss, and taklif (obligation). For each, consider why they disprove the idea of purpose benefiting creation.
3
Understand the Mu'tazila connection
Sanusi mentions the Mu'tazila who hold that Allah must do what is most beneficial (al-aslah). This refutation is directed against them.
4
Reflect on the counter-argument
If someone says the purpose is reward, Sanusi replies that Allah could grant reward without any obligation or action.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

وهو أنَّ المصلحة التي قُدِّرَ أَنَّ الفعلَ خُلِقَ لأجلِها إِنَّما تعودُ إلى المخلوقِ ، لا إليهِ تعالى - فهو باطل أيضاً ؛ لأنَّهُ لو كان تعالى يبعثه على الفعل إيصال المصالح للعباد.. لكـانَ مراعاة الصلاح والأصلح لهم واجباً عليه عقلاً كما تقوله المعتزلة ، وهو ظاهرُ البطلان ، وسيأتي دليلُ بطلانِهِ في باب ما يجوز في حقه تعالى(۱) وأقرب شيء يدلك على بطلانه : إيلام الله تعالى للأطفال والبهائم ، ولا صلاحَ لهم في ذلك قطعاً ، وإِنْ قُدِّرَ ثَمَّ مصلحةٌ فهو قادر أن يوصلها بغير إيلام ، وكذا تخليد عذابِ الكافرِ معَ مساواتِهِ للمؤمن المخلَّدِ في النعيم مع أنَّ كلاً منهما لا أثرَ لهُ في شيءٍ مِنْ أفعاله ، وكذا تكليفُ الخلْقِ في الدنيا ؛ أي : لا مصلحة لهم فيهِ فإنْ قالوا : حصول عظيمِ الثواب لهم على تكليف المشاق . قلنا : لا أثر لهم في شيءٍ مِنْ تلك الأفعالِ ، فاستوى مَنْ فعلَ ومَنْ لم يفعل وأيضاً : فمولانا جل وعلا قادر على إيصال ذلكَ الثواب العظيمِ لهم بغير تكليف ولا فعل لهم أصلاً ومن أدلَّةِ إِبطالِ تعليل أفعالِ اللهِ تعالى وإرادته بالأغراض : أنَّ الغرضَ إِمَّا أنْ يكونَ قديماً ، فيلزم قدمُ الفعل ، وإلا كانَ الباري جلَّ وعلا ناقصاً ؛ لفواتِ غرضِهِ ، أو حادثاً ، فيحتاج هذا الغرضُ في
His statement: 'And that His will is not for a purpose' means: it is impossible for His will – exalted is He – to be for the purpose of bringing any act into existence or annihilating it for any purpose; i.e., there is no cause for any act such that the cause impels Him – exalted is He – to bring an act into existence or annihilate it. Rather, He – majestic and exalted – is free to choose in both matters. [Proof of the falsity of His acts being for a purpose] He argued for this point in the Creed: that the purpose for which the act is supposed to have been done is either a benefit returning to Him – exalted is He – or a benefit returning to His creation. The first is false for two reasons: One: it entails that His high essence undergoes new events, and this is false as will come in the section on life (1). Two: it entails that He – majestic and exalted – is deficient in His high, self-sufficient essence and becomes perfect through His acts, because the perfection of that benefit would have been missed by Him on this assumption before the creation of the act with which it came, and missing perfection is deficiency, and He – exalted is He – is free of that by consensus of the rational people. And [?], or the bag and the incapacity, for it contains that the acquisitions of servants are also by decree. 289 (1) It will come (p. 331). As for the second division – that the benefit for which the act is supposed to have been created only returns to the creature, not to Him – exalted is He – it is also false; because if He – exalted is He – were impelled to the act by conveying benefits to servants, then observing what is beneficial and most beneficial for them would be intellectually obligatory upon Him, as the Mu'tazila say, and that is clearly false; and its proof of falsity will come in the chapter on what is possible concerning Him – exalted is He (1). And the closest thing indicating its falsity is Allah's inflicting pain on children and animals, and there is absolutely no benefit for them in that; and if a benefit is supposed, He is able to convey it without inflicting pain. Similarly, the eternal punishment of the disbeliever despite his similarity to the believer who is eternally in bliss, while each of them has no effect on anything of His acts. Similarly, the obligation of creation in this world; i.e., there is no benefit for them in it. If they say: the attainment of great reward for them is due to the obligation of hardships. We say: they have no effect in any of those acts, so the one who does and the one who does not are equal. Also: our Master – majestic and exalted – is able to convey that great reward to them without obligation or any act on their part. Among the proofs of invalidating the attribution of purposes to Allah's acts and His will: that the purpose is either eternal, then the eternity of the act follows, otherwise the Creator – majestic and exalted – would be deficient due to the loss of His purpose; or temporal, then this purpose needs...
Source pages p. 289

Commentary
This passage contains a proof from Sanusi that Allah's will and acts cannot be for any purpose (gharad). He begins by stating the claim: it is impossible for Allah's will to be for any purpose, meaning there is no cause (illa) impelling Him to act. Then he provides the proof. He divides the supposed purpose into two kinds: (1) a benefit returning to Allah Himself, and (2) a benefit returning to His creation. He refutes both. For the first kind, he gives two arguments: (a) it would entail that Allah's essence undergoes new events (tajaddud al-hawadith), which is false; (b) it would entail that Allah is deficient and becomes perfect through His acts, because the benefit would have been missed before the act, implying deficiency – which is impossible by consensus. For the second kind (benefit to creation), he argues that if Allah were impelled by conveying benefits, then observing what is beneficial (al-salah) and most beneficial (al-aslah) would be obligatory upon Him, as the Mu'tazila hold – but that is false. He gives concrete examples: Allah inflicts pain on children and animals with no benefit, and if any benefit were supposed, Allah could convey it without pain. Similarly, eternal punishment of disbelievers and eternal bliss of believers are not based on any effect of their actions. Also, obligation (taklif) itself yields no benefit; if the reward is the purpose, Allah could grant it without any obligation. Finally, he adds another proof: the purpose must be either eternal (qadim) or temporal (hadith). If eternal, the act would also be eternal (impossible for temporal acts). If temporal, the purpose itself would need a cause, leading to infinite regress. The text cuts off mid-sentence at the page end, but the argument is clear.

Key Terms
مصلحة
صلاح
أصلح
تكليف

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some hold that Allah must always do what is most beneficial for His creation (al-aslah), as the Mu'tazila claim.
Correction: Sanusi shows this is false by pointing to real examples where no benefit is apparent, and Allah is free to act without obligation.

Checkpoint
Which example does Sanusi use to argue against the second kind of purpose?
A) The resurrection
B) Inflicting pain on children and animals
C) The creation of the universe
D) The sending of prophets

Key Takeaway
Allah's acts are not for the benefit of creation; doing so would imply obligations on Allah that are false.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
If Allah's actions are not for your benefit, why do you still obey Him? Does your obedience come from love or from expectation of reward? How would your worship change if you knew that your actions do not bring you any benefit?
?
Consider the example of pain inflicted on children and animals. If Allah has no purpose in that pain, how do you reconcile that with your own sense of justice? Does this lesson challenge you to trust His wisdom even when you see no reason? What would it take for you to submit completely?
?
The lesson says Allah could grant you reward without any obligation. Knowing that He chose to command you anyway, what does that say about your response to His commands? Are you grateful that He gave you a path, or do you resent the effort? What must change in your heart?

Divine Attributes
intermediate
5 min
No Purpose: The Eternal/Temporal Argument
Sanusi argues that any purpose is either eternal or temporal, both impossible.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the logical argument that a purpose for Allah's acts must either be eternal or temporal, both of which lead to absurdity.

Core Idea
Any purpose for Allah's acts is either eternal (leading to eternal act) or temporal (needing another purpose), both impossible.

Sanusi provides a final argument against purposes for Allah's acts: the purpose itself must be either eternal or temporal. If eternal, the act must also be eternal (false for temporal acts). If temporal, the purpose needs a cause, leading to infinite regress. Although the passage is cut off, the argument is clear and powerful.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage
Read the Arabic and translation. Note the two possibilities: the purpose is qadim (eternal) or hadith (temporal).
2
Follow the first branch
If the purpose is eternal, the act must be eternal. But many acts are temporal, so this is false. Also, if the purpose is eternal and the act is not, then Allah would be deficient for not fulfilling His eternal purpose – impossible.
3
Follow the second branch
If the purpose is temporal, it must have a cause, which would be another purpose – leading to infinite regress or an eternal purpose, contradicting itself.
4
Appreciate the argument's strength
This is a classic kalam argument that cuts off the possibility of any purpose for divine acts, regardless of its nature.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله : ( وأنْ تكونَ إرادته لا لغرض له ) يعني : أنَّه يستحيلُ أنْ تكونَ إرادته تعالى لإيجادِ فعلِ مِنَ الأفعال أو إعدامه لغرض مِنَ الأغراض ؛ أي: لا علةَ لشيءٍ مِنَ الأفعال بحيث تكون العلة تبعثه تعالى على إيجاد فعل أو إعدامه ، بل هو جلَّ وعلا مختار في كلا الأمرين . 1 دليل بطلان أن تكون أفعاله تعالى لغرض ] واستدل على هذا المطلب في العقيدة : بأن الغرض الذي يُقدَّرُ أنَّ الفعل كانَ لأجله ؛ إما أن يكون مصلحة تعود إليه تعالى ، أو مصلحةً تعود لخلقه والأوَّلُ باطل لوجهين : أحدهما : استلزامُهُ أنْ تكونَ ذاتُهُ العليَّةُ تتجدَّدُ عليها الحوادث وهو باطل ؛ لما سيأتي في فضل الحياة (١) الثاني : استلزامهُ أنْ يكونَ تعالى جلَّ وعزَّ ناقصاً في ذاتِهِ العليَّةِ الغنيَّةِ ، ويتكمَّلَ بأفعالِهِ ؛ لأنَّ كمالَ تلكَ المصلحة قد فاتته على هذا الفَرْضِ قبلَ خلْقِ الفعل الذي وُجِدَتْ معَهُ ، وفوتُ الكمال نقص وهو تعالى مُنزَّه عنه بإجماع العقلاء والكيس [؟] ، أو الكيس والعجز » ، ففيه أن أكساب العباد بقدر أيضاً ۲۸۹ • (۱) سيأتي ( ص ۳۳۱ ) وأما القسم الثاني - The Sunni Interpretation of Juristic Causal Language
Sanusi explains the Sunni interpretation of juristic causal language.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Sunni scholars interpret causal language in scriptures as signs or favors, not as necessitating causes.

Core Idea
Sunni juristic causes are signs, not causes that obligate Allah.

This lesson clarifies a key point of Sunni theology: when jurists speak of 'causes' for rulings, they are not claiming that Allah is obligated by reason. Instead, they refer to signs or favors. Let's read Sanusi's precise explanation.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once, then the literal translation. Notice the terms 'amaraat' and 'masaalih'.
2
Identify the two interpretations
The passage gives two meanings for 'causes': signs (amaraat) arbitrarily placed by Sharia, or interests (masaalih) observed as favor. Find them in the text.
3
Contrast with Mu'tazila view
The passage explicitly denies the Mu'tazila interpretation that causes impel the Sharia. Understand why this distinction matters for divine attributes.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
توجيه النصوص الشرعية المشتملة على تعليل الأحكام والأفعال. وقوله: ( وما يوجد من التعليل لذلك في كلام أهل الشرع ... ) إلى آخره؛ يعني: أن ما يذكره فقهاء أهل السنة من علل الأحكام لا يفهم على ظاهره من العلة الباعثة للشرع على الحكم كما تقوله المعتزلة، بل مرادهم منها: الأمارات التي نصبها الشرع بمحض الاختيار، أو أرادوا بها: المصالح التي راعاها الشرع مع تلك الأحكام تفضلاً منه، لا على طريق الوجوب العقلي.
Directing the scriptural texts that contain causation of rulings and acts. And His statement: (And what is found of causation for that in the speech of the people of the Sharia ...) to its end; meaning: what the jurists of Ahl al-Sunna mention of causes of rulings is not to be understood on its apparent meaning as the cause that impels the Sharia to the ruling, as the Mu'tazila say, but their intended meaning from them is the signs (amaraat) which the Sharia set up by pure choice, or they intended by them the interests (masaalih) which the Sharia observed along with those rulings as a favor from Him, not by way of rational obligation.
Source pages p. 294

Commentary
This passage is an interpretation (tawjeeh) of scriptural texts that appear to ascribe causes to divine rulings. The author clarifies that when Sunni jurists speak of 'causes' (ilal), they do not mean a genuine cause that necessitates the ruling (as Mu'tazila claim). Rather, they refer either to 'signs' (amaraat) arbitrarily placed by the Sharia, or to 'interests' (masaalih) that Allah bestows as favor—not due to any rational obligation. The key is that knowledge of good and evil does not come from reason alone.

Key Terms
تعليل
علة
أمارات
مصالح
وجوب عقلي

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Phrases like 'because of this reason' imply Allah is rationally obligated to act.
Correction: Sunni interpretation denies any necessitating cause; causes are merely signs or favors, not obligations.

Checkpoint
How does the author interpret the 'causes' (ilal) mentioned by Sunni jurists?
A) They are true causes that make the ruling rationally obligatory.
B) They are signs (amaraat) set by choice, or favors (masaalih).
C) They are independent sources of obligation.
D) They have no meaning.

Key Takeaway
Sunni jurists use causal language to mean signs or divine favor, not rational necessity.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you say 'Allah commands this because it is good for me,' do you feel He is obliged to benefit you? How does this lesson's emphasis on pure favor challenge your entitlement to divine rewards?
?
Imagine you are about to disobey a command because you cannot see any benefit in it. Does knowing that the command is based on Allah's pure choice, not on any reason you can grasp, make you more or less willing to submit? What does your reaction reveal about your heart?
?
Consider one ruling in Islam that you struggle with because you think it seems harmful or pointless. Can you now see that your judgment of harm or benefit is irrelevant? Will you repent from judging Allah's command and accept it as a sign of His favor, even without understanding?

The Ta'wil of the Lam in 'Liya'budun' and the Final Cause
Sanusi explains the metaphorical interpretation of the lam in 'liya'budun' and defines the final cause.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the lam in 'liya'budun' must be interpreted metaphorically as a lam of causality, and how the concept of final cause is applied metaphorically to God's commands without implying motivation.

Core Idea
God's actions are never motivated by external purposes; all metaphors affirm dependence, not need.

This lesson examines a key Quranic verse (51:56) that might seem to suggest God created humans for a purpose—worship. Sanusi shows that such an interpretation would contradict God's self-sufficiency. He explains that the lam of purpose must be understood metaphorically, comparing God's command to a final cause only in terms of dependency, not motivation.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage for this lesson once, then read the translation. Notice how the passage begins by refuting the Mu'tazila view and then presents the metaphorical interpretation.
The passage includes a definition of final cause from philosophy.
2
Identify the problem with literal reading
The literal reading of 'liya'budun' implies God acts for a purpose (worship). Sanusi argues this is impossible because all events are by God's will and He is not motivated.
3
Understand the final cause metaphor
Sanusi explains that the lam is metaphorical, comparing the command to worship to a final cause. A final cause is something that motivates action by its conception, but here it only indicates dependency, not motivation.
The metaphor is a derivative metaphor (isti'ara taba'iyya).
4
Reflect on the limitation
Sanusi stresses that the comparison is only in 'absolute dependence' (mujarrad al-tarattub), not in 'motivation' (ba'th), because God cannot be motivated by anything external.
This preserves God's transcendence.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
التقدير أيضاً : وما أردتُ بخلْقِ الجن والإنس إلا العبادة ، على أصلهم الفاسد أيضاً في أنَّ إرادة الله تعالى على وَفْقِ أمرِه ، فما لا يأمرُ بهِ مِنَ الكفر والمعاصي فليس بمرادٍ له عندهم . وإذا حقَّقْتَ : أَنَّ الحوادث كلَّها إنَّما وقعَتْ بخَلْقِ اللهِ تعالى ، لا أثرَ لَمَنْ سواه في شيءٍ منها .. عرفت وجوب عموم إرادته تعالى لجميعها ، كما وجب عموم قدرتِهِ تعالى لها ، فتعالى أن يكون في ملكه ما لا يريدُ
ويصح أن تجعل اللام في قوله تعالى : ( ليعبدون ) للتعليل مجازاً على طريق الاستعارةِ التبعيَّةِ ؛ بأنْ يُشَبَّهَ الأمرُ بالعبادة في ترتُبِ وجودِهِ على خلقِ الجن والإنس.. بالعلَّةِ الغائية في ترتب وجودها على معلولها (۱) ، فأُدخِلَتْ لام التعليل على العبادة لتدل على ذلك والعلة الغائبة في الاصطلاح : هي ما يبعث بحسَبِ تصورِهِ على
فعل شيءٍ وإِنْ كانَ يتأخَرُ وجودُهُ على ذلك الشيء ؛ كالربح للتجارة ؛ فإنَّهُ علَّةٌ غائيَّةٌ لها ؛ إذْ هو الحامل باعتبار تصوره على التلبس بالتجارة وإن كان في الوجودِ يتأخَّرُ عنها
وبالجملة : فالعلَّةُ الغائيَّةُ هي فائدةُ الشيء ، وهي أبداً تتقدم ذهناً وتتأخَّرُ وجوداً في الخارج ، وهي التي تريد الفلاسفة بقولهم : أَوَّلُ الفكر آخرُ العمل (۲) ، فهذهِ العلَّةُ الغائيَّةُ الباعثةُ بها شُبِّهَ الأمرُ بالعبادة
في مطلق الترتب على الشيء ، حتى أُدخِلَتْ لام التعليل عليه(۱) ، لا في البعث عليه ؛ لاستحالة أن يبعث مولانا جلَّ وعزَّ على الفعل شيء كما عرفت .
ونحو هذه الآيةِ في وجوبِ التأويل : قوله تعالى : ﴿ وَجَزَيْهُم بِمَا صَبَرُوا جَنَّةً وَحَرِيرًا ﴾ [الإنسان : ۱۲] لأنَّ إعطاءهُ تعالى الجنَّةَ لَمَنْ يَشَاءُ إِنَّما هو بمخضِ فضلِهِ ، ولا سببَ لذلك عقلاً ؛ إذ الأعمال السابقة في الدنيا هي مخلوقة لهُ تعالى بغيرِ واسطةٍ مِنَ العبد أصلاً ، فلم يخترع أحدٌ مِنَ المخلوقاتِ فعلاً مِنَ الأفعالِ حتى يستحق عقلاً أنْ يُثاب عليه أو يُعاقَبَ ، لكنَّ تلك الأفعال لمَّا كانَتْ أماراتٍ شرعيَّةً على ما اختار سبحانه وتعالى مِنَ التفضُّل بالثواب أوِ العدلِ بالعقاب.. أُطلق عليها السببيَّةُ لذلكَ على طريقِ المجازِ .
وقس على هذا ما لا ينحصر في الكتاب والسنَّةِ وكلام الأئمةِ مِنَ الظواهر ، والله سبحانه هو الموفّقُ بفضلِهِ ، وهو الهادي مَنْ يَشَاءُ إِلى صراط مستقیم
The estimation also: 'And I did not create jinn and humans except to worship' (Quran 51:56) — according to their corrupt principle also that the will of God Most High is in accordance with His command, so what He does not command of disbelief and sins is not willed by Him according to them. And if you realize that all events occur only by the creation of God Most High, and there is no effect for anyone else in any of them, then you know the necessity of the universality of His will for all of them, just as the universality of His power for them is necessary. So far exalted is He above having in His dominion what He does not will. And it is valid to make the lam in His saying 'liya'budun' (so that they worship) a lam of causality metaphorically, by way of derivative metaphor; such that the command to worship is compared, in the dependence of its existence on the creation of jinn and humans, to the final cause in the dependence of its existence on its effect. So the lam of causality was inserted onto worship to indicate that, not to indicate motivation. And the final cause in technical terminology is that which, by its conception, impels one to do something, even if its existence is later than that thing; like profit for trade, for it is a final cause for it, since it is the motivator by virtue of its conception to engage in trade, even if it comes later in existence. In summary, the final cause is the benefit of a thing, and it always precedes in the mind and comes later in existence externally, and it is that which the philosophers refer to by their saying: 'The first thought is the last action.' It is this final cause — the motivator — with which the command to worship was compared in the absolute dependence on the thing, so that the lam of causality was inserted onto it, not in the motivation, because it is impossible that anything motivates our Lord, Majestic and Exalted, to act, as you have understood. And similar to this verse in the necessity of interpretation is His saying: 'And We have rewarded them for their patience [with] a garden and silk' (Quran 76:12). Because God's giving of the Garden to whomever He wills is only by the essence of His grace, and there is no rational cause for that; since the preceding deeds in this world are created by Him Most High without any intermediary from the servant at all, so no creature has invented any action from among actions such that he rationally deserves to be rewarded or punished for it. But since those actions were legal indicators of what He, Glorified and Exalted, has chosen of favor with reward or justice with punishment, causality was attributed to them metaphorically. And analogize to this what is beyond count of apparent meanings in the Book, the Sunna, and the words of the imams. And God, Glorified, is the Grantor of success by His grace, and He is the Guide of whom He wills to a straight path.
Source pages p. 296

Commentary
This passage is a theological explanation of the necessity of ta'wil (interpretation) for two Quranic verses that might seem to imply that God's actions are motivated by purposes or that human actions cause reward. The author begins by referring to a previous discussion about the verse 'And I did not create jinn and humans except to worship Me' (Quran 51:56). He states that according to the corrupt principle of the Mu'tazila, God's will is in accordance with His command, so what He does not command (like disbelief and sins) is not willed by Him. But the correct view is that all events occur by God's creation, and no one else has any effect. Therefore, God's will and power encompass all things; He is exalted above having in His dominion what He does not will. Then the author says one can interpret the lam in 'liya'budun' as a lam of causality (lam al-ta'lil) metaphorically, through a derivative metaphor (isti'ara taba'iyya). This means that the command to worship is compared to a final cause (al-illa al-gha'iyya) in terms of the dependence of the commanded act (worship) on the creation of jinn and humans. But this comparison is only in terms of dependency, not motivation, because God is not motivated by any external purpose. He defines the final cause in philosophical terms: it is that which, by its conception, impels one to act, even if its existence is later than the act, like profit for trade. In summary, the final cause is the benefit of a thing; it precedes in the mind but comes later in external existence. The philosophers refer to it by the saying 'the first thought is the last action.' It is this final cause — the motivator — with which the command to worship is compared, but only in the aspect of absolute dependency (mujarrad al-tarattub), not in the aspect of motivating God, because it is impossible that anything motivates God. Then the author gives another verse requiring similar interpretation: 'And We have rewarded them for their patience with a garden and silk' (Quran 76:12). Because God's giving of the Garden to whomever He wills is purely by His grace, and there is no rational cause for that; the preceding deeds in this world are created by God without any intermediary from the servant, so no creature has invented an act to be rationally deserving of reward or punishment. However, since those actions are legal indicators (amarat shar'iyya) of what God has chosen of favor with reward or justice with punishment, the term 'cause' (sabab) is applied to them metaphorically. The author concludes that one should analogize to this all the apparent meanings in the Book, the Sunna, and the words of the imams.

Key Terms
التأويل
التعليل
الاستعارة التبعية
العلة الغائية
الإرادة
القدرة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The lam in 'liya'budun' literally means that God created humans for the purpose of worship, implying God benefits from worship.
Correction: Sanusi shows the lam is metaphorical; it only indicates that worship depends on creation, not that God has a purpose or need.

Checkpoint
According to Sanusi, in what aspect is the command to worship compared to a final cause?
A) In the aspect of motivation
B) In the aspect of absolute dependence
C) In the aspect of creation
D) In the aspect of will

Key Takeaway
The lam in 'liya'budun' is metaphorical; it shows that worship depends on creation, not that God needs worship.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
If God does not act for any purpose, why do you still think your worship benefits Him? How does that change how you approach your prayer today?
?
You claim to believe in God's absolute self-sufficiency, but do you sometimes feel guilty as if God 'needs' your worship? What would it mean to truly worship without attributing any need to Him?
?
'The first thought is the last action' — but not for God. When you plan your acts of worship, do you treat them as if they somehow complete or benefit Him? What would it mean to serve a Being who is utterly complete without you?

Divine Attributes
intermediate

The Metaphorical Interpretation of Reward for Patience and Legal Indicators
Sanusi interprets the reward verse metaphorically and introduces the concept of legal indicators.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the Quranic verse about rewarding patience (Quran 76:12) must be interpreted metaphorically, and how human actions are merely legal indicators (amarat shar'iyya) of God's grace, not causes of reward.

Core Idea
Human actions do not cause reward; they are only signs of God's preordained favor.

Another verse that might suggest causality is the reward for patience: 'We have rewarded them for their patience with a garden and silk.' This could imply that patience earns paradise. Sanusi explains that since all actions are created by God, no act truly deserves reward. The attribution of causality to actions is metaphorical; they are just indicators of God's choice.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic for this lesson (starting from 'ونحو هذه الآية') and its translation. Notice how the argument builds from the premise that God creates all actions.
The passage also includes a general methodological instruction at the end.
2
Understand the impossibility of rational causality
Sanusi states that God's giving of the Garden is purely by His grace, and there is no rational cause. Human actions are created by God without intermediary, so no creature deserves reward or punishment rationally.
3
Learn the concept of legal indicators
Actions are called 'causes' only metaphorically because they are legal indicators (amarat shar'iyya) of what God has chosen (favor or justice). This preserves God's sole agency.
4
Apply the principle broadly
Sanusi concludes that all apparent causal language in the Quran, Sunna, and imams' words should be interpreted similarly: metaphorically, as indicators of God's will, not real causes.
This is a key methodological principle in Ash'ari theology.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Good deeds like patience deserve paradise as a reward for the action itself.
Correction: Sanusi shows that no action can rationally earn reward; patience is only a sign (indicator) that God has chosen to grant paradise by His grace.

Checkpoint
According to Sanusi, why is the attribution of causality to human actions in the reward verse considered metaphorical?
A) Because actions are created by God and thus cannot be causes
B) Because the verse uses figurative language
C) Because the reward is only in the afterlife
D) Because patience is not a real action

Key Takeaway
Human actions are not causes of divine reward or punishment; they are merely legal indicators of God's gracious choice.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you read 'We have rewarded them for their patience,' what feeling stirs in you? Do you sense a claim to deserve something from God? Now that you know patience is only a sign, not a cause—does that make your obedience feel more humble or less motivated?
?
If your best deeds are not the reason God grants you Paradise, then what is the real purpose of striving in worship? How does this truth reshape the way you approach your daily prayers, fasting, and charity—especially when you feel you have 'earned' something?
?
Think of a time you felt entitled to God's reward because of your effort. In light of this lesson, can you honestly let go of that entitlement? Will you live today as if every breath of faith is a gift, not a paycheck? What would change if you truly believed that both your obedience and your salvation are pure grace?

The Proof for God's Knowledge from Creation's Perfection
Sanusi begins the proof that the Originator of the world must be omniscient.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the rational proof for God's omniscience based on the intricate perfection of the world, as argued in the third section of Sanusi's text.

Core Idea
The world's intricate design necessitates a knowing, eternal Creator.

Sanusi now turns to establish a third divine attribute: knowledge. He argues that the world's perfection—its fine craftsmanship and wondrous secrets—cannot be the product of an ignorant maker. This lesson presents the beginning of that argument, grounding God's knowledge in the observable order of creation.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and identify the claim
Read the Arabic passage once, then the translation. Notice the claim: the Originator of the world must be knowing because of the world's intricate design.
The passage is cut off; we only have the start of the argument.
2
Understand the two preliminary clarifications
Before the proof, Sanusi clarifies two points: (1) God's knowledge is neither necessary nor acquired; (2) it encompasses all rational judgments. These are premises from earlier discussions.
3
Analyze the design argument
The proof: the world contains 'fine points of craftsmanship and wondrous secrets' which logically imply a knowing maker. This is a rational necessity, not mere empirical observation.
Consider why an ignorant maker could not produce such perfection.
4
Trace the implications for eternity and scope
Sanusi adds that this knowledge must be eternal (like power), free from defect, and encompassing all rational judgments. Otherwise, it would require a specifier or become temporal, which is absurd.
These points reinforce the attribute's perfection.
5
Note the incomplete ending
The passage ends with an explanatory note that is cut off. The conclusion of the proof will appear in the next lesson. Reflect on what has been established so far.
This is a good stopping point for reflection.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الفضل الثالث
في وجوب علمه تعالى، وما يتعلق به
مراده بما يتعلَّق بهِ: ما ذكرَهُ مِنْ تنزه العلم عن الاتصاف بكونه ضرورياً أو نظرياً، وما ذكرَهُ مِنْ وجوب تعلقِهِ بما لا نهاية لهُ مِنْ جميع
ما صدقتْ عليهِ الأحكام العقلية
[دليل ثبوتِ صفة العلم له تعالى وبيانُ تعلقاتِها]
ويلزمُ أنْ يكونَ مُحدِثُ العالم عالماً لما احتوى عليه العالمُ مِنْ دقائقِ الصنع وعجائب الأسرارِ، وأن يكون ذلك بعلم قديم؛ لما سبق في القدرة، متنزه عن الضرورة والنظرِ، وإلا قارنَهُ الضرر، أو كان حادثاً، ويتعلق أقسام الحكم العقلي، وإلا لزم الافتقار إلى المخصص كما
سبق
بجميع
يعني: أنَّه لو لم يكنْ مُحدِثُ العالم عالماً.. لم يكن كلُّ فردٍ مِنْ أفراد العالم منصفاً بما لا يُحاط بهِ مِنْ أنواع المحاسن ودقائقها التي
The Third Section: On the Obligatoriness of His Knowledge, Exalted, and What Pertains to It.
His meaning by 'what pertains to it': what he mentioned of the transcendence of knowledge from being described as necessary or acquired, and what he mentioned of the obligation of its encompassing that which has no end from all that is subject to rational judgments.
[Demonstration of the establishment of the attribute of knowledge for Him, Exalted, and explanation of its objects.]
It necessarily follows that the Originator of the world is knowing, because of what the world contains of fine points of craftsmanship and wondrous secrets, and that this is by eternal knowledge; because of what preceded in [the discussion on] power, transcendent over necessity and acquisition; otherwise, harm would accompany it, or it would be temporal, and it encompasses the divisions of rational judgment; otherwise, dependence on a specifier would follow as before.
By all:
Meaning: If the Originator of the world were not knowing... then every individual of the individuals of the world would not be characterized by what cannot be encompassed of the types of beauties and their fine details which...
Source pages p. 298

Commentary
This passage opens the third major section (al-faḍl al-thālith) of the work, which deals with the attribute of God's knowledge (ʿilm). The author first clarifies what he means by 'what pertains to it': namely, two points already established: (1) that God's knowledge is neither necessary (ḍarūrī) nor acquired (naẓarī) — it is transcendent beyond these categories; and (2) that it necessarily encompasses all infinite objects of rational judgment. Then he presents a proof (dalīl) for the existence of this attribute. The proof is based on the world’s design: the world contains intricate craftsmanship (daqāʾiq al-ṣunʿ) and amazing secrets (ʿajāʾib al-asrār), which clearly indicate a knowing maker. Furthermore, this knowledge must be eternal (qadīm), as argued previously regarding power; it cannot be temporal (ḥādith) or subject to necessity or acquisition, as that would entail imperfection. It must also encompass all rational judgments (aqsām al-ḥukm al-ʿaqlī); otherwise it would be dependent on a specifier (mukhaṣṣiṣ), which would be absurd. The passage ends with an explanatory note: 'Meaning: If the Originator of the world were not knowing... then every individual of the world would not be characterized by what cannot be encompassed...' — this seems to be a brief restatement of the argument, but it cuts off due to the page break. The overall argument is a standard kalām proof from design for God's omniscience.

Key Terms
علم
قديم
محدث العالم
دقائق الصنع
ضروري
نظري
حكم عقلي

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: That the proof for God's knowledge relies solely on empirical observation rather than rational necessity.
Correction: The author emphasizes that the perfection of the world logically necessitates a knowing creator; it is a rational argument, not mere induction.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what does the world's 'fine points of craftsmanship and wondrous secrets' necessitate?
A) That the world is eternal.
B) That the Originator of the world is knowing.
C) That the world was created by chance.
D) That human beings can acquire perfect knowledge.

Key Takeaway
The intricate design of the world rationally necessitates a Creator who is knowing, eternal, and whose knowledge encompasses all rational judgments.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you examine the 'fine points of craftsmanship' in your own body—the complexity of DNA, the precision of the eye—does your heart truly acknowledge that the Creator knows every detail? Or do you still live as if your life's events are random, ignoring His complete awareness? What practical step can you take today to align your thoughts with this truth?
?
The passage teaches that God's knowledge is neither learned nor instinctive—it is absolutely unique. Reflect on a moment when you acted as if your own limited knowledge was sufficient, doubting God's wisdom. How does humbling yourself before His transcendent knowledge change the way you approach your uncertainties?
?
If God’s knowledge encompasses all rational judgments, then nothing in your life escapes His awareness—not your hidden sins, your silent prayers, or your future struggles. Are you living as if you truly believe this? What would change in your daily routine if you constantly remembered that every thought and action is known by the All-Knowing?
