The Necessity of God's Knowledge from the World's Design
Sanusi argues from general design to a knowing Creator.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Sanusi uses the intricate design of the world to prove that the Creator must possess necessary, eternal knowledge, and why attributing the world's wonders to chance is irrational.

Core Idea
The world's intricate order demands a knowing Creator; chance is irrational.

Sanusi begins by establishing that God's knowledge is necessary (واجب) and not acquired (نظري) nor merely necessary (ضروري) in the sense of being forced. He then presents a rational argument: the world is filled with subtle craftsmanship and wonders that no human mind can fully grasp. If the Creator were ignorant, such wonders could not exist except by chance, but that violates rational necessity. Anyone who claims otherwise has left the realm of rational discourse.

Guided Path
1
Read the opening definitions
Read the Arabic passage from 'الفضل الثالث' to 'الأحكام العقلية'. Understand what Sanusi means by knowledge's transcendence and its relation to rational judgments.
Note that knowledge here is an eternal attribute, not acquired.
2
Follow the logical argument
Sanusi argues that the world's intricate craftsmanship necessitates a knowing originator. This knowledge must be eternal, as established in the discussion of power. Trace the logic: if the creator were ignorant, harm would follow or knowledge would be temporal.
The words 'يلزم' and 'وإلا' indicate necessary implications.
3
Understand the consequence of denial
Sanusi explains that if someone attributes the wonders of the world to chance from an ignorant being, they are obstinate and outside the realm of rational people. Reflect on why this is a strong reductio ad absurdum.
He uses the term 'عنداء للحق' and 'جاحداً للضرورة'.

Source Text

الفضل الثالث
في وجوب علمه تعالى ، وما يتعلق به
مراده بما يتعلَّق بهِ : ما ذكرَهُ مِنْ تنزه العلم عن الاتصاف بكونه ضرورياً أو نظرياً ، وما ذكرَهُ مِنْ وجوب تعلقِهِ بما لا نهاية لهُ مِنْ جميع ما صدقتْ عليهِ الأحكام العقلية

ثم يلزم أن يكون محدث العالم عالماً لما احتوى عليه العالم من دقائق الصنع وعجائب الأسرار، وأن يكون ذلك بعلم قديم؛ لما سبق في القدرة، متنزه عن الضرورة والنظر، وإلا قارنه الضرر، أو كان حادثاً، ويتعلق أقسام الحكم العقلي، وإلا لزم الافتقار إلى المخصص كما سبق بجميع.

يعني: أنه لو لم يكن محدث العالم عالماً .. لم يكن كل فرد من أفراد العالم منصفاً بما لا يحيط به من أنواع المحاسن ودقائقها التي تعجز العقول عن الإحاطة بأدناها، ومن جوَّز صدور تلك العجائب مع كثرتها وخروجها عن حد الحصر من الجاهل على سبيل الاتفاق .. كان معانداً للحق، جاحداً للضرورة، وسقطت مكالمته؛ لخروجه عن حيز العقلاء.

ومن تأمل الإنسان الذي نسبته إلى سائر العالم كلا شيء، ونظر أصغر عضو من أعضائه كعينه مثلاً .. اطلع في ذلك على العجب العجاب، فليتأمل فيها كيف جعلها الله تعالى في أعلى رأسه وفي مقدمه؛ ليرى بها القاصي والداني، ولم يجعلها بارزة في ظاهر وجهه كما فعل في أنفه، بل وضعها سبحانه في زاوية منه؛ ليقل وصول الأذى إليها؛ لما هي عليه من شدة القبول له عادة لرطوبتها وصقالتها وصفائها، ونحو ذلك من الصفات التي ركبها الله تعالى عليها.

ثم إنه تعالى جعل عليها غشاءين لحميين رطبين ناعمين ينطبقان عليها ويصقلانها، ويكنسان ما يتعلق بها من الهباء الذي لو اجتمع فيها لتضررت به عادة، والإنسان في هذا يحركها بقدرة الله تعالى وإرادته انفتاحاً وانغلاقاً على ممر الأوقات وتوالي الساعات، من غير قصد ولا ارتياد، ولا تكلف في غالب عموم الحالات.

ثم إنه تعالى بتمام حكمته جعل في طرفي كل واحد منهما شعراً منسوجاً صفيقاً بطول كل واحدة منهما، ومدَّه منهما إلى خارجهما مداً.
The Third Section: On the necessity of His knowledge, may He be exalted, and what pertains to it. His meaning by 'what pertains to it': that which he mentioned of the transcendence of knowledge from being described as necessary or theoretical, and what he mentioned of the necessity of its relating to what has no end from all that the rational judgments apply to. Then it follows that the originator of the world must be knowing, because of what the world contains of subtleties of craftsmanship and wonders of secrets, and that this be by eternal knowledge, as preceded in [the discussion of] power, transcendent over necessity and speculation, otherwise harm would accompany it, or it would be temporal, and it relates to the categories of rational judgment, otherwise the need for a specifier would follow as preceded, in all respects. Meaning: that if the originator of the world were not knowing, then every individual of the world would not be adorned with what cannot be encompassed of the types of beauties and their subtleties which minds are incapable of encompassing their least part. And whoever considers the possibility of the occurrence of those wonders, despite their multiplicity and their being beyond enumeration, from an ignorant [being] by chance, would be an obstinate opponent of truth, a denier of the necessary, and his discussion would be invalid because of his departure from the realm of rational people. And whoever reflects upon man, whose proportion to the rest of the world is like nothing, and looks at the smallest organ of his organs, like his eye for example, will come to know in that an amazing wonder. So let him reflect on how God, may He be exalted, made it in the highest part of his head and in its front, so that he may see with it the far and the near, and did not make it protruding on the surface of his face as He did with his nose, but rather placed it, glorified be He, in a corner of it, so that harm would arrive to it less, because of what it has of strong receptivity to it, customarily, due to its moisture, polish, and clarity, and similar attributes which God, may He be exalted, composed upon it. Then He, may He be exalted, placed upon it two fleshy, moist, soft coverings that close over it and polish it, and sweep away what attaches to it of dust which, if it accumulated in it, would harm it customarily. And man, in this, moves them by the power of God and His will, opening and closing over the passage of times and the succession of hours, without intention or deliberation, nor effort in most general states. Then He, may He be exalted, by the perfection of His wisdom, made on the edges of each one of them woven thick hair, the length of each one of them, and extended it from them outward.
Source pages p. 298

Commentary
This passage is a proof for the attribute of divine knowledge (العلم). It begins with the title statement that God's knowledge is necessary (واجب) and clarifies what is meant by 'what pertains to it': that knowledge is neither necessary (ضروري) nor acquired (نظري) in its essence, and that it relates to an infinite number of things. Then the proof is presented: the world contains intricate craftsmanship and wonders that could only come from a knowing creator. This knowledge must be eternal (قديم), as argued in the section on power, and it transcends necessity and speculation. If the creator were ignorant, it would be impossible for such wonders to occur by chance, as that would go against rational necessity. The passage then provides a detailed example: the human eye. Despite man being a tiny part of the universe, his eye is a marvel. God placed it in the highest front of the head for optimal vision, not protruding like the nose to avoid injury. It has eyelids (غشاءين لحميين) that clean and protect it, moving involuntarily by God's power. It also has eyelashes (شعراً منسوجاً) that filter dust. All these details demonstrate perfect wisdom and design, proving that the Creator is all-knowing. The argument follows the kalam method of using empirical observation to deduce divine attributes.

Key Terms
العلم
الضرورة
النظر
الاتفاق
الحكمة
العقلاء

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think the world's order could have arisen by pure chance without a knowing creator.
Correction: Sanusi argues that the vast complexity and beauty of the world cannot be the result of chance, as that would contradict rational necessity. To claim otherwise is to reject the obvious.

Checkpoint
According to Sanusi, what follows if the originator of the world were not knowing?
A) The world would still exist but without beauty.
B) Every individual of the world would not be adorned with its beauties and subtleties.
C) Knowledge would be acquired through reason.
D) The originator would be ignorant by choice.

Key Takeaway
The world's intricate design logically demands a Creator with eternal, non-acquired knowledge; denying this is irrational.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you observe the intricate design of your own body or the natural world, do you genuinely attribute it to the wisdom of a knowing Creator, or do you sometimes fall into the casual assumption that 'things just happen'? What small shift in your daily awareness could change that?
?
The passage calls someone who attributes the world's wonders to chance an 'obstinate opponent of truth' and 'outside the realm of rational people.' Have you ever excused your own lack of reflection on creation by saying 'it's just nature' or 'it evolved' without considering the intelligent cause behind it? Will you commit, starting today, to pause and consciously affirm the knowledge of Allah when you see order and beauty?
?
Sanusi argues that God's knowledge is eternal and encompasses all rational judgments. If you truly believe that Allah knows everything, including your hidden thoughts and future choices, does your relationship with Him reflect that reality? In what area of your life are you living as if He does not see or know, and what concrete step will you take to align your actions with this belief?

The Design of the Human Eye as Evidence
Sanusi uses the human eye's anatomy as a detailed example of divine design.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the specific design of the human eye—its placement, eyelids, and eyelashes—demonstrates the Creator's perfect knowledge and wisdom.

Core Idea
Every detail of the eye, from its location to its protection, shows infinite wisdom.

After presenting the general argument, Sanusi offers a concrete example that anyone can reflect upon: the human eye. Despite man being a tiny part of the universe, the eye is a marvel of design. Its position at the front and top of the head, its protective eyelids, and its filtering eyelashes all point to a wise and knowing Creator.

Guided Path
1
Read the description of the eye's placement
Read the passage from 'ومن تأمل الإنسان' to 'شدة القبول له عادة'. Notice how the eye is placed in the front and top of the head for optimal vision, and not protruding like the nose.
Sanusi emphasizes that the eye is set in a recess to reduce harm.
2
Understand the function of the eyelids
Read the description of the two fleshy coverings (eyelids) that close over the eye, clean it, and protect it. Note that they move involuntarily by God's power.
The eyelids clean dust and keep the eye moist.
3
Reflect on the eyelashes and overall wisdom
Read the final part about the thick woven hair (eyelashes) on the edges. Consider how all these features together demonstrate perfect wisdom and knowledge.
The eyelashes filter dust and foreign objects.

?

?
If the tiniest detail of your eye—like the eyelashes—is a deliberate act of divine wisdom, how much more deliberate are the major events of your life? How should this truth change the way you face your next trial or decision?

Divine Wisdom in the Eye's Design
Describes the eyelids, lashes, tears, and lens as divine wisdom

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the detailed design of the eye's protective parts points to a wise Creator.

Core Idea
Every part of the eye is precisely designed for its purpose.

This passage examines the human eye not as a mere biological organ but as a masterpiece of divine craftsmanship. Imam Sanusi walks through each protective layer—eyelids, lashes, tears, and lens—showing how each is tailored to its function. As we read, pay attention to how he ties each design feature to a specific benefit, and how this cumulative evidence leads to the conclusion of a wise Creator.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without analyzing. Then read the translation. Notice the sequence of structures: eyelids, eyebrow/lashes, tears, lens.
The passage uses a descriptive style with contrasts.
2
Identify each protective structure and its purpose
List the four features mentioned: upper/lower eyelids, eyebrow/lashes, salty tears, central lens. For each, note the specific benefit stated in the text.
The benefits are prevention of dust, blocking harm, preservation from decay, and clarity of vision.
3
Notice the contrasts and the word 'customarily'
The author contrasts salty tears (eye) with sweet water (mouth) and repeatedly says 'عادَةً' (customarily). Why does he emphasize typical conditions? Think about how this strengthens the argument from design.
The design is suited to normal circumstances, showing foresight.
4
Reflect on the argument from design
How does this piece-by-piece description build a case for a wise Creator? Consider that each part has a specific function that benefits the eye. The cumulative evidence points to intentional design, not chance.
This is a classic teleological argument in kalam.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
على قَدْرِ حاجتِهما إليهِ عادةً ؛ فالفَوقانيُّ منهما يمنعُ ما يأتي مِنَ الهباء إلى العينِ مِنْ جهةِ العلوّ ، والآخرُ منهما يمنع أيضاً مثل ذلك مما يلي مِنَ جهةِ السُّفْل
وجعل تعالى أيضاً عليها مثلَ دُرْبُوزِ فوقها ، وعلى حاجبها شَعَرُ
منسوج أيضاً لمَّا كانَ كثرةُ الأذى إنَّما يأتي مِنْ فوقها عادةً .
ثم أمدَّها تعالى بماء مالح ملازم لها ؛ لحفظها عند ذلك ، وقلة قبولها العفنَ معَهُ عادةً ، بخلافِ ما جعلَ في فيهِ مِنَ العين النبَّاعَةِ بماءٍ عَذْبِ دائماً
وجعلَ أيضاً محلَّ الإدراكِ منها في وسطها ؛ وهو القَدْرُ الذي يرى
فيهِ الناظرُ إِليهِ نفسَهُ لشدَّةِ صفائِهِ وصقالتِهِ .
According to the measure of their need for it customarily; so the upper one of them prevents what comes from dust to the eye from the direction of above, and the other one also prevents similar from what comes from the direction of below. And He, exalted, also made upon it something like a projection above it, and on its eyebrow hair woven also because the abundance of harm comes from above it customarily. Then He supplied it with salty water attached to it, for its preservation thereby, and its little acceptance of decay with it customarily, unlike what He placed in its mouth of the gushing eye with constantly sweet water. And He also made the place of perception from it in its center; and it is the amount in which the beholder sees himself in it due to its intensity of clarity and polish.
Source pages p. 300

Commentary
This passage is a descriptive proof of divine wisdom in the eye's design. The author explains that God has given the eye protective structures proportionate to its needs. He first mentions the eyelids: the upper prevents dust from above, the lower from below. Then the eyebrow with woven hair (lashes?) to block harm from above. Then the salty tears to preserve the eye from decay, contrasting with the sweet water of the mouth. Finally, the lens is placed in the center because it is the clearest point for perception. The author follows the anatomical order, showing each part's function. He emphasizes that all these are by God's design ('وجعل تعالى') and that they are measures according to custom ('عادةً'). This is a classic kalam argument from teleology: the intricate design implies a wise Creator.

The Seven Layers and Three Humors of the Eye
Describes the seven layers and three humors as evidence of wise design.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the eye's seven layers and three humors, each with specific benefits, point to deliberate creation and divine wisdom.

Core Idea
The eye's layers and humors are arranged with specific benefits, proving intentional design.

The theologian continues to explore the eye, now detailing its structure—seven distinct layers arranged in a specific order and three humors of different consistencies. Each part has a unique benefit that would not be achieved with a random arrangement. This passage builds the case for a Wise Creator through the complexity and specificity of creation.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic and translation. Notice the words 'طبقات سبع' (seven layers) and 'رطوبات ثلاث' (three moistures). Pay attention to the phrase 'مع كل واحدة مصلحة' (with each one a benefit).
2
Identify the two main parts
The passage has two parts: first the seven layers, then the three humors. Note that the author stresses that each layer is different and each has a unique benefit not found elsewhere.
The benefit is tied to the specific arrangement.
3
Reflect on the argument
Ask yourself: Why does the theologian emphasize that the benefit is not found in other arrangements? This points to deliberate design, not chance.
This is a classic teleological argument.

And likewise He made in it three moistures of different states; among them the thick, and among them the thin, and among them the intermediate.

Commentary
This passage continues the description of the eye's creation. The author states that God made the eye of seven distinct layers, each differing from the other, and arranged them in a specific order. Each layer has a unique benefit that is not found in any other layer or in a different arrangement. Then he mentions three humors (moistures) of varying consistency: thick, thin, and intermediate. The emphasis is on the specificity and order, which indicates deliberate design. The phrase 'مصلحة لا توجد عادة في غيرها' (a benefit not found in others) reinforces the idea that each part has a purpose. This is a classic argument from complexity and specificity to a wise Creator.

The optic nerve and the wisdom of its curved path
Describes the optic nerve's delicate structure and its curved shape to prevent harm

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the specific design of the optic nerve demonstrates divine wisdom and why its curved shape is necessary to protect the eye.

Core Idea
The optic nerve's curve is a precise design that prevents harm, showing Allah's wisdom.

The author presents an even more remarkable example of divine craftsmanship: the optic nerve. This extremely delicate structure carries a subtle substance from the brain to the eye. The great wisdom lies in its curved path, which ensures the eye is not harmed by a direct, forceful flow. Let's examine this argument.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once, then the translation. Notice the author's description of the optic nerve's thinness and hollowness.
Pay attention to the phrase 'تضعف القوى البشرية عن الإحاطة بها' – human powers fall short of encompassing this wisdom.
2
Identify the problem
The author explains what would happen if the substance flowed straight into the eye. Understand why this would be harmful.
The text says 'لأضرَّتْها عادةً' – it would customarily harm it.
3
Understand the divine solution
See how God made the tube curved and bent to slow the flow. This prevents harm.
Key words: 'منعطفاً ومنعرجاً' – bent and curved.
4
Reflect on the theological implication
This intricate design points to a wise Creator. Consider how the author uses this as evidence for divine wisdom.
The author concludes with 'إلى غير ذلك من الحكم' – there are many such wisdoms.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأعجبُ مِنْ هذا كلّه وألطفُ : ما أمدَّها الله تعالى بهِ مِنَ الدماغ ؛ وهو المسمى على أوضاع الطبائعيِّينَ : القوَّةَ الباصرةَ ؛ وذلكَ أنَّ في العينِ عِرْقاً متصلاً منها إلى الدماغ أرقَ مِنْ خيط العنكبوتِ ، لا يراه إلا حاد البصر جداً جداً ، وهو مُجوَّفٌ تنصب فيهِ مِنَ الدماغ مادَّةٌ إلى العينِ تصغُرُ عن أنْ تُرى للطافتِها ورقّتِها ، وفي هذا العِرْقِ حكمة تضعفُ القوى البشريَّةُ عن الإحاطة بها ؛ وذلكَ أنَّ هذه المادة التي تنزلُ في هذا الأنبوب المجوَّفِ لو نزلَتْ بحدّتِها على صوب واحد إلى العينِ معَ ما فيها مِنَ اللطافة والتغذية والرخاوة وسرعة القبول.. لأضرَّتْها عادةً ، فجعلَ مولانا جل وعزَّ بقدرته وحكمته التي لا تحيطُ العقولُ بأدنى شيء منها .. هذا الأنبوب الذي تنصب فيه القوى منعطفاً ومنعرجاً انعراجاً يمنع سرعة الانصباب وحدَّتَهُ ، فلا تصلُ المادَّةُ إلى العينِ مِنْ أجلِ ذلكَ إلا على مَهَلٍ ، بلا حدة ولا قوة انصباب ولا أذيَّةٍ ، إلى غير ذلكَ مِنَ الحِكَمِ التي لو حاولنا عدها لخرجنا بذلك عن غرض الكتابِ
And more wondrous than all of this and more subtle: what God supplied it with from the brain; which is called, according to the conventions of the naturalists, the power of sight. And that is because in the eye there is a vein connected from it to the brain, thinner than a spider's thread, which none sees except one extremely sharp-sighted. And it is hollow; into it flows from the brain a substance to the eye, too small to be seen due to its subtlety and fineness. And in this vein is a wisdom that human powers fall short of encompassing. And that is because this substance that descends in this hollow tube, if it descended with its directness upon one direction to the eye, with what is in it of subtlety, nourishment, softness, and quick acceptance, it would harm it customarily. So Our Master, exalted and mighty, by His power and wisdom that minds do not encompass even the least thing of them, made this tube into which the powers descend bent and curved, a bending that prevents rapidity of flow and its force. So the substance does not reach the eye except gradually, without force or power of flow or harm. Besides other wisdoms which if we attempted to count them we would depart by that from the purpose of the book.
Source pages p. 300

Commentary
This passage presents a specific example of divine wisdom in the optic nerve. The author says this is even more wondrous and subtle than the previous structures. He describes a vein (the optic nerve) that connects the eye to the brain, extremely thin. It is hollow and carries a subtle substance from the brain to the eye. The wisdom is that if this substance flowed directly and swiftly, it would harm the eye due to its delicacy. So God made the tube curved and bent, slowing the flow to protect the eye. The author emphasizes that this is beyond human comprehension ('تضعف القوى البشرية عن الإحاطة بها'). He also notes that there are many such wisdoms that cannot be enumerated within the scope of this book. The passage is a classic kalam argument: a specific, intricate mechanism that could not have arisen by chance, pointing to a wise Creator.

عرق

منعرج
انصباب

Correction: The passage explains that the optic nerve is intentionally curved to slow the flow of substance from the brain, preventing harm to the eye.

Wisdom in the Eye's Nerve Design
Sanusi describes the optic nerve's winding as proof of divine wisdom.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the structure of the optic nerve demonstrates divine wisdom and why human intellect cannot fully comprehend all such wisdoms.

Core Idea
The eye's curved nerve proves God's wisdom, not random chance.

Sanusi presents a remarkable example of bodily design to illustrate divine wisdom. He describes the optic nerve's winding path and explains why this is no accident but a deliberate act of creation. This passage invites us to see the hand of God in the most subtle details of our own bodies.

Guided Path
1
Read and understand the passage
Read the Arabic passage once, then read the literal translation. Focus on the anatomical description Sanusi provides.
The passage is dense with descriptive detail; take your time.
2
Visualize the anatomy
Sanusi describes the optic nerve as extremely sharp (ḥād al-baṣar), hollow, and containing a delicate substance from the brain. Picture this structure to grasp the argument.
3
Identify the problem
If the substance flowed straight and quickly, it would harm the eye due to its sharpness and the eye's delicacy. This is a potential design flaw that wisdom avoids.
The key phrase: 'for would harm it normally'.
4
See the divine solution
God made the nerve curved (munʿatij) and winding (munʿarij), which slows the substance's descent, preventing harm. This reveals purposeful design.
5
Reflect on the lesson's scope
Sanusi ends by noting that listing all such wisdoms would exceed the book's purpose, implying human limitations. This is a humbling reminder.
Connect to the learning objective: wisdom beyond full comprehension.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
حاد البصر جداً جداً ، وهو مُجوَّفٌ تنصب فيهِ مِنَ الدماغ مادَّةٌ إلى العينِ تصغُرُ عن أنْ تُرى للطافتِها ورقّتِها ، وفي هذا العِرْقِ حكمة تضعفُ القوى البشريَّةُ عن الإحاطة بها ؛ وذلكَ أنَّ هذه المادة التي تنزلُ في هذا الأنبوب المجوَّفِ لو نزلَتْ بحدّتِها على صوب واحد إلى العينِ معَ ما فيها مِنَ اللطافة والتغذية والرخاوة وسرعة القبول.. لأضرَّتْها عادةً ، فجعلَ مولانا جل وعزَّ بقدرته وحكمته التي لا تحيطُ العقولُ بأدنى شيء منها .. هذا الأنبوب الذي تنصب فيه القوى منعطفاً ومنعرجاً انعراجاً يمنع سرعة الانصباب وحدَّتَهُ ، فلا تصلُ المادَّةُ إلى العينِ مِنْ أجلِ ذلكَ إلا على مَهَلٍ ، بلا حدة ولا قوة انصباب ولا أذيَّةٍ ، إلى غير ذلكَ مِنَ الحِكَمِ التي لو حاولنا عدها لخرجنا بذلك عن غرض الكتابِ
The optic nerve is extremely sharp, and it is hollow: a substance from the brain flows down through it to the eye, too small to be seen due to its delicacy and thinness. In this vein there is a wisdom that human powers are too weak to fully comprehend; and that is that if this substance which descends in this hollow tube were to descend with its sharpness in one direction to the eye, along with what it contains of delicacy, nutrition, softness, and quick receptivity, it would normally harm it. So our Lord, Exalted and Majestic, by His power and wisdom, of which minds cannot comprehend the least part, made this tube through which the powers flow curved and winding, a bending that prevents speed of flow and its sharpness, so the substance does not reach the eye thereby except gradually, without sharpness, force of flow, or harm, besides other wisdoms which if we attempted to count them we would thereby depart from the purpose of the book.
Source pages p. 301

Commentary
This passage provides a detailed anatomical description of the optic nerve (ḥād al-baṣar) as a proof of divine wisdom (ḥikma). Sanusi argues that the nerve is hollow, allowing a subtle substance from the brain to flow to the eye. If this substance descended straight and quickly, it would harm the eye due to its sharpness and the eye's delicate nature. However, God made the nerve curved and winding, slowing the flow to prevent harm. This design shows that the eye's structure is purposeful, not accidental, and that human intellect cannot fully grasp all the wisdoms behind creation. Sanusi ends by noting that listing all such wisdoms would exceed the book's scope.

Divine Wisdom in Creation Refutes Natural Causality
Sanusi argues that diversity in animal eyesight proves divine choice and refutes natural causality.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the diversity in animal eyesight and other created marvels establish that all effects are by divine choice, not by natures or causes.

Core Idea
Creation's diversity proves Allah's will, not natural causes.

This passage presents a powerful rational argument: the variety in animal eyesight and other wonders cannot be explained by natures (طبائع) or causes (علل). Instead, they demonstrate that Allah creates each thing with specific benefits by His pure choice. As you read, notice how Sanusi uses a concrete example (the cat) to dismantle the idea of inherent causality.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic passage and its translation
Read the full Arabic passage once, then its translation. Let the argument flow naturally before analyzing details.
2
Identify the main claim and the example
Sanusi begins by stating that Allah differentiated animal eyesight according to need. He then gives the example of the cat seeing in darkness and day. Why is this example effective?
The cat's ability contradicts the idea that vision depends solely on light or physical structures.
3
Understand the refutation of natural causality
Sanusi explicitly denies that sight is produced by nature (طبع), cause (علة), light rays, eye structure, or brain. He asserts that any prevention is by Allah's pure choice. Note the repeated denial.
This refutes both ancient theories (rays from the eye) and philosophical naturalism.
4
Reflect on the broader argument and conclusion
Sanusi suggests that if we considered all wonders in animals, heavens, plants, etc., the proof would be overwhelming. Even a small portion suffices to affirm Allah's wisdom, knowledge, power, and will.
The passage ends with praise and a supplication, emphasizing gratitude and human limitation.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ثم إنَّ مولانا سبحانه خالف بين أعين الحيوانات على قدر الحاجة إليها وتمام الانتفاع بها ، وليزول بذلك أوهام القائلين بالطبائع والعللِ ؛ فجعلَ تعالى الهرَّةَ تبصرُ في الظلام والنهار لتكمل بذلك منفعتها التي اختار سبحانه وتعالى أن يخلقها معها ، وكذا الكلابُ والبغال والخيلُ والحمير والجمالُ ؛ حتى يتمكَّنَ الإِنسانُ مِنَ السفرِ بها ليلاً ونهاراً ، وليعلمَ المُوفَّقُ أَنَّ رؤية الرائي ليسَتْ حاصلةً عن طبْعِ ولا علة، وليسَتْ بانبعاث أشعَةٍ مِنَ العين ، ولا مشروطاً فيها خصوص حاسة العين ، ولا طبقاتها السبع ، ولا استمدادها بما تستمدُّ به مِنَ الدماغ، ولا يمنعُ منها قُرْب ولا بُعْد، ولا حجاب مِنْ ظلمة أو غيرها، وما اتفقَ أَنْ يُوجَدَ معَ تلك الأمورِ مِنَ المنع فإنَّما هو بمخض اختيار الله تعالى أنْ يُوجد ذلك عندَها لا بها ، فتبارك الله أحسنُ الخالقين .

ولو شرعنا في تركيب الحيواناتِ واختصاص كلّ واحدٍ منها بما هو مُعَدُّ له ، وما خلق مولانا جل وعزَّ في السماواتِ مِنَ العجائب والنجومِ السيارة والبطيئةِ ، واختلافِ أقدارها وحُسْنِ وضعِها ، واختلافِ الأهوية والأزمنة لأجل الأوقات ، واختلاف أنواع النباتِ والأشجارِ بحسَبِ الأزمنة والأمكنة ، والثمار ونموّها في بعضها وإثمارها في غيرِه ، وبلوغها ، وتمام المنفعة.. لخُضْنا بذلك في بحرٍ لا يُشقُ تبَّارُه ، وبرٌ لا يُطاقُ غباره
·
وعند إدراكِكَ هذا النزر اليسير الذي أشرنا إليهِ مِنْ عجائب مُلْكِ مولانا جل وعزَّ . . لا يعسر عليك دعوى الضرورة في إثباتِ حِكْمَتِهِ جلَّ وعزَّ ، وعظيمٍ علمِهِ ، وباهرِ قدرتِه ، ونفوذ إرادته ، فسبحانَ القهَّارِ الذي أعمى كثيراً مِنَ البصائر عن مشاهدة هذه العجائب معَ كثرتها وعموم ظهورها ! وله الحمد جلَّ وعزَّ وعظيم الشكر في تعليمِ ما شاءَ مِنْ ذلك بمحْضِ فضلِهِ
·
لَكَ الْحَمْدُ مَوْلانَا عَلَى كُلِّ نِعْمَةٍ وَمِنْ جُمْلَةِ النَّعْمَاءِ قَوْلِي لَكَ الْحَمْدُ فَلا حَمْدَ إِلَّا أَنْ تَمُنَّ بِنِعْمَةٍ تَعَالَيْتَ لَا يَقْوَىٰ عَلَى حَمْدِكَ الْعَبْدُ
Then indeed our Lord, glorified is He, differentiated between the eyes of animals according to the extent of need for them and complete benefit from them, and so that the illusions of those who claim natural dispositions and causes might be removed. So He, exalted, made the cat see in darkness and daytime so that its benefit might be completed thereby, which He, glorified and exalted, chose to create with it. Likewise dogs, mules, horses, donkeys, and camels, so that man might be able to travel with them by night and day, and so that the one granted success might know that the sight of the seer is not produced by a nature nor a cause, nor by the emission of rays from the eye, nor conditioned by the particular sense of the eye, nor its seven layers, nor its derivation from what it derives from the brain, nor does proximity or distance prevent it, nor a veil of darkness or anything else. And whatever prevention happens to be found with those things is only by the pure choice of Allah, exalted, that it should exist in conjunction with them, not by them. So blessed is Allah, the best of creators.
·
And if we were to undertake [discussing] the composition of animals and the specialization of each one of them for what it is prepared for, and what our Lord, majestic and mighty, created in the heavens of wonders, the planets and fixed stars, and the differences of their magnitudes and the beauty of their arrangement, and the differences of climates and times for the sake of the seasons, and the differences of types of plants and trees according to the seasons and places, and fruits and their growth in some and their fruiting in others, and their ripening, and complete benefit, we would thereby plunge into a sea whose depths cannot be reached, and a land whose dust cannot be endured.
·
And when you perceive this small amount which we have indicated of the wonders of the kingdom of our Lord, majestic and mighty, it will not be difficult for you to claim necessity in proving His wisdom, majestic and mighty, and the greatness of His knowledge, and the brilliance of His power, and the penetration of His will. So glorified is the Subduer who has blinded many of the inner insights from observing these wonders despite their abundance and general manifestation! And to Him be praise, majestic and mighty, and great thanks for teaching whatever He wills of that by pure grace.
·
To You be praise, our Lord, for every blessing, and among the blessings is my saying to You: praise, so there is no praise unless You bestow a blessing; exalted are You, the servant is not able to praise You.
Source pages p. 302

Commentary
This passage is an argument for divine wisdom and against natural causality. It begins by stating that Allah made differences in animal eyesight according to need and benefit, to remove the illusions of those who believe in natures (طبائع) and causes (علل). It gives the example of the cat seeing in darkness and day, and lists other animals like dogs, horses, etc., so that humans can travel with them day and night. The point is that sight is not produced by nature, cause, light rays, the physical eye or its layers, or the brain, and is not prevented by distance, darkness, or any veil. If any prevention occurs alongside these factors, it is by Allah's pure choice — the prevention occurs at the time of these factors not because of them. Then the author praises Allah as the best of creators. He then says that if he were to go into the details of animal composition, celestial wonders, climates, plants, etc., it would be an endless task. He concludes that perceiving even a small portion of these wonders makes it necessary to affirm Allah's wisdom, knowledge, power, and will. He ends with a supplication and praise.

Key Terms
طبائع
علل
اختيار
رؤية
شعاع

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Natural causes independently produce effects such as sight.
Correction: Sanusi argues that sight is not produced by nature, cause, or physical factors; it is solely by Allah's choice. What appears as causation is divine accompaniment.

Checkpoint
According to Sanusi, why did Allah make the cat see in both darkness and light?
A) Because cats have a natural disposition for night vision
B) So that its benefit might be completed by Allah's choice
C) Because the eye emits rays that allow vision in the dark
D) To prove that vision depends on the brain

Key Takeaway
All created diversity, from animal eyesight to celestial wonders, points not to natural causality but to the direct, wise choice of Allah.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you look at the night sky or the variety of animals, do you truly see the hand of Allah or do you take them for granted? Will you pause today and thank Him for one specific detail you noticed?
?
If you sincerely believe that nothing happens except by Allah's choice, then why do you still panic over outcomes as if they depend on natural causes? What is the root of that fear?
?
Allah has 'blinded many of the inner insights from observing these wonders.' Are you among the blind? When you fail to see His wisdom in your trials, what does that reveal about your trust in His choice?

Two Proofs that Divine Knowledge is Eternal
Sanusi gives two proofs that God's knowledge is eternal.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand two rational proofs establishing that Allah's attribute of knowledge is eternal and cannot be temporally originated.

Core Idea
God's knowledge cannot be temporal; otherwise, absurdities like eternal ignorance or infinite regress would follow.

Sanusi presents two concise proofs from the Matn that defend the eternity of divine knowledge. These arguments show that if knowledge were created in time, it would contradict the evidence of creation and lead to a vicious regress. Understanding these proofs strengthens one's conviction about God's timeless attributes.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without analysis. Then read the full translation. Let the author's words sink in before interpreting.
Note the section heading: 'قدم صفة العلم له تعالى'.
2
Analyze the first proof (opposite argument)
Identify the first proof: if knowledge were temporal, its opposite (ignorance) would be eternal. Since the eternal cannot cease, God would never have knowledge—but creation shows He does. So knowledge must be eternal.
This is a reductio ad absurdum argument.
3
Analyze the second proof (regress argument)
Examine the second proof: if knowledge were temporal, it would require prior knowledge to intend its creation, leading to an infinite regress. Since regress is impossible, knowledge must be eternal.
The concept of تسلسل (infinite regress) is central here.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قدم صفة العلم له تعالى
·
قوله : ( بعلم قديم ؛ لما سبق في القدرة ) يعني : أنَّ كونَ العالِمِ عالماً مِنْ غيرِ علم يقوم به لا يُعقل ، والدليل على كون ذلك العلم قديماً : أنَّه لو كانَ حادثاً لكانَ ضدُّهُ مِنَ الجهل ونحوه قديماً ، والقديم لا ينعدم ، فيلزمُ ألا يتّصف بالعلم أبداً ، ومصنوعاته تشهد بكذبِ ذلك
·
وأيضاً : فلو كانَ العلم حادثاً لاحتاجَ في إحداثِهِ إلى علم آخر يتعلَّقُ بهِ ؛ إذ القصد إلى إحداثِهِ فرعُ العلم به ، ثم ننقل الكلام إلى العلم الآخرِ ، فيحتاجُ هو أيضاً في إحداثِهِ إلى علم آخر ، ويلزمُ التسلسل
Presentation of the attribute of knowledge for Him, exalted.
·
His statement: 'By eternal knowledge, as preceded in the case of power' means: that the knower being knowing without knowledge subsisting in him is not intelligible. And the proof that that knowledge is eternal is that if it were temporal, then its opposite from ignorance and the like would be eternal, and the eternal does not cease to exist, so it would entail that He would never be described with knowledge, and His creations bear witness to the falsity of that.
·
And also: if knowledge were temporal, then in its origination it would need another knowledge related to it, because the intention to originate it is a branch of knowledge of it. Then we transfer the discussion to the other knowledge, and it also in its origination would need another knowledge, and infinite regress would follow.
Source pages p. 303

Commentary
This passage is a proof that God's attribute of knowledge is eternal. It starts with the section heading 'Presentation of the attribute of knowledge for Him, exalted.' Then it explains a statement from the matn: 'By eternal knowledge, as preceded in the case of power.' The author says this means that it is inconceivable for a knower to be knowing without knowledge subsisting in him. Then he gives two proofs:

1) If knowledge were temporal (حادث), then its opposite (ignorance and similar) would be eternal, because the opposite of a temporal thing is eternal. But the eternal does not cease to exist, so God would never be described with knowledge. However, His creations testify that He has knowledge, so this is false.

2) If knowledge were temporal, its origination would require another knowledge that relates to it, because intending to originate something requires prior knowledge of it. Then that other knowledge would also need another knowledge, leading to an infinite regress (تسلسل), which is impossible.

Thus, knowledge must be eternal.

Key Terms
علم قديم
حادث
ضد
تسلسل

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think that God's attribute of knowledge could be temporal or acquired.
Correction: The proofs show that if knowledge were temporal, it would entail either eternal ignorance (impossible) or infinite regress (impossible), so it must be eternal.

Checkpoint
What is a consequence if God's knowledge were temporal according to the first proof?
A) He would need to learn from creation
B) His ignorance would be eternal and He would never have knowledge
C) He would have multiple knowledges
D) The universe would be eternal

Key Takeaway
God's knowledge is eternal because temporal knowledge would lead to the impossibility of eternal ignorance or an infinite regress.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
If God's knowledge is eternal and encompasses everything, does your belief in that change how you live today? What would it mean to act as if every thought and action is already known by the Eternal? Will you take one step today to align your heart with that reality, such as praying with more presence or repenting of a hidden sin?
?
The proof shows that temporal knowledge would require an infinite regress, but God's knowledge is simple and without need. How does this simplicity of God's attribute confront your tendency to overcomplicate your faith or rely on your own understanding? Can you trust that His knowledge is sufficient, even when you don't understand your circumstances?
?
Your own knowledge is limited and acquired; God's is not. Does that distinction humble you or make you feel distant? Choose one area of your life where you have been relying on your own knowledge instead of seeking His guidance through revelation. What will you do today to seek His eternal knowledge instead?

Reflection on the Wonders of God's Kingdom
Reflective exhortation leading to necessary attributes.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how reflecting on the wonders of creation leads to a necessary conviction of God's wisdom, knowledge, power, and will.

Core Idea
Witnessing creation's wonders makes God's attributes self-evident.

This passage serves as a reflective interlude in Sanusi's text. After having alluded to some marvels of God's kingdom, the author invites the reader to contemplate even a small portion of these wonders. He asserts that such contemplation leads to an immediate and necessary recognition of God's key attributes — His wisdom, knowledge, power, and will. This is not a logical proof but an appeal to direct insight.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic and English to feel the devotional tone. Notice the flow from wonder to praise.
The poetry at the end continues the theme of praise.
2
Identify the attributes mentioned
Find the four attributes: wisdom (حكمة), knowledge (علم), power (قدرة), and will (إرادة). Notice they are presented as resulting from contemplation.
3
Reflect on the method
Understand that the argument is not demonstrative but based on 'necessity' (ضرورة) – an immediate recognition. Ask yourself why the author uses the word 'لا يعسر' (it is not difficult).
This connects to the idea of self-evident truths in kalam.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وعند إدراكِكَ هذا النزر اليسير الذي أشرنا إليهِ مِنْ عجائب مُلْكِ مولانا جل وعزَّ . . لا يعسر عليك دعوى الضرورة في إثباتِ حِكْمَتِهِ جلَّ وعزَّ ، وعظيمٍ علمِهِ ، وباهرِ قدرتِه ، ونفوذ إرادته ، فسبحانَ القهَّارِ الذي أعمى كثيراً مِنَ البصائر عن مشاهدة هذه العجائب معَ كثرتها وعموم ظهورها ! وله الحمد جلَّ وعزَّ وعظيم الشكر في تعليمِ ما شاءَ مِنْ ذلك بمحْضِ فضلِهِ · لَكَ الْحَمْدُ مَوْلانَا عَلَى كُلِّ نِعْمَةٍ وَمِنْ جُمْلَةِ النَّعْمَاءِ قَوْلِي لَكَ الْحَمْدُ فَلا حَمْدَ إِلَّا أَنْ تَمُنَّ بِنِعْمَةٍ تَعَالَيْتَ لَا يَقْوَىٰ عَلَى حَمْدِكَ الْعَبْدُ(۱)
And upon your perception of this small amount that we indicated from the wonders of the kingdom of our Master, may He be glorified and exalted, it is not difficult for you to claim necessity in proving His wisdom, may He be glorified and exalted, and the greatness of His knowledge, and the brilliance of His power, and the penetration of His will. So glorified is the Subduer who has blinded many insights from witnessing these wonders despite their abundance and widespread manifestation! And to Him belongs praise, may He be glorified and exalted, and great thanks for teaching whatever He willed of that by His pure grace. 'Praise be to You, our Master, for every blessing, and among the blessings is my saying: Praise be to You. For there is no praise unless You bestow a blessing. Exalted are You, the servant cannot suffice for praising You.' (1)
Source pages p. 303

Commentary
This passage is a reflective exhortation, not a formal proof. The author addresses the reader after having presented some marvels of God's kingdom (presumably in preceding pages). He states that realizing even a small portion of these wonders makes it easy to assert with necessity (dharura) the existence of God's wisdom, knowledge, power, and will. This is an appeal to immediate recognition (badiha) rather than a demonstrative argument. The author then praises God for hiding these wonders from some, and thanks Him for teaching what He wills. The two lines of poetry express similar sentiments: all praise is from God, and human praise is itself a gift from Him. The passage serves as a devotional interlude emphasizing the self-evident nature of God's attributes through creation.

Key Terms
حِكْمَة
عِلم
قُدرة
إرادة
ضرورة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what does perceiving the wonders of God's kingdom lead to?
A) Doubt about God's existence
B) Necessity in proving God's attributes
C) Proof that God's knowledge is created
D) Denial of God's power

Key Takeaway
Perceiving even a glimpse of the wonders of God's creation makes His attributes of wisdom, knowledge, power, and will self-evidently necessary.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When was the last time you truly paused to wonder at something in creation—not just pass by it? If you cannot remember, what does that say about your awareness of God's attributes?
?
The passage says God has blinded many insights from seeing these wonders. Are you among those whose hearts have become dull to the signs around you? What would it take to restore that vision?
?
You claim to believe in God's wisdom and power—but do your daily actions reflect that conviction? When you face a difficulty, do you act as if God's power is all-encompassing, or do you panic as if you are alone? What change must you make to live in light of His greatness?

Proof from the Opposite: God's Knowledge Must Be Eternal
First argument for the eternity of God's knowledge from the impossibility of opposite.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the first kalam proof that God's knowledge is eternal, based on the impossibility of its opposite being eternal.

Core Idea
If God's knowledge were created, ignorance would be eternal, which is false.

Before we can accept that God's knowledge is eternal, we must see why it cannot be temporal. The author argues that if knowledge were originated, its opposite—ignorance—would have been eternal, and what is eternal cannot cease, so God would never know. But creation shows He does. Let's unpack this concise argument.

Guided Path
1
Understand the starting claim
Read the opening sentence: the author clarifies that being knowing requires an attribute of knowledge subsisting in God. This is the foundation.
2
First proof: opposite argument
Follow the conditional: if knowledge were originated, then ignorance would be eternal. Why? Because before knowledge, there is absence of knowledge (ignorance), and that absence would be eternal since only God is eternal. Eternal cannot cease, so He would never have knowledge. But creation shows He knows.
This is a reductio ad absurdum.
3
Reflect on the conclusion
The proof shows that God's knowledge must be eternal (qadim), not originated.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله : ( بعلم قديم ؛ لما سبق في القدرة ) يعني : أنَّ كونَ العالِمِ عالماً مِنْ غيرِ علم يقوم به لا يُعقل ، والدليل على كون ذلك العلم قديماً : أنَّه لو كانَ حادثاً لكانَ ضدُّهُ مِنَ الجهل ونحوه قديماً ، والقديم لا ينعدم ، فيلزمُ ألا يتّصف بالعلم أبداً ، ومصنوعاته تشهد بكذب ذلك وأيضاً : فلو كانَ العلم حادثاً لاحتاجَ في إحداثِهِ إلى علم آخر يتعلَّقُ بهِ ؛ إذ القصد إلى إحداثِهِ فرعُ العلم به ، ثم ننقل الكلام إلى العلم الآخرِ ، فيحتاجُ هو أيضاً في إحداثِهِ إلى علم آخر ، ويلزمُ التسلسل
His saying: 'With eternal knowledge, as was stated previously concerning power' means that the fact that the knower is knowing without knowledge subsisting in him is not rationally conceivable. And the proof that this knowledge is eternal is that if it were originated (hadith), then its opposite, ignorance and the like, would be eternal, and the eternal does not cease to exist, so it would necessitate that He would never be described with knowledge, and His creations testify to the falsehood of that. Also: if knowledge were originated, then in originating it, it would need another knowledge pertaining to it, because the intention to originate it is a branch of knowledge of it. Then we would transfer the discussion to the other knowledge, and it too would need another knowledge in its origination, and an infinite regress (tasalsul) would follow.
Source pages p. 303

Commentary
This passage is a commentary on a line from Sanusi's text that states God has 'eternal knowledge'. The author first explains the meaning: it is inconceivable for a knower to be knowing without an attribute of knowledge subsisting in him. Then he gives two proofs for the eternity of God's knowledge.
First proof: If God's knowledge were originated (created in time), then its opposite—ignorance—would have been eternal (because before knowledge existed, the absence of knowledge would be ignorance, and that absence would be eternal). The eternal cannot cease to exist, so God would never acquire knowledge, but creation shows that He knows.
Second proof: If knowledge were originated, the act of originating it would require prior knowledge of the intended act (since intention is based on knowledge). That prior knowledge would then need to be originated, requiring another knowledge, leading to an infinite chain (tasalsul), which is impossible. Therefore, God's knowledge must be eternal.

Key Terms
علم قديم
حادث
ضد

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some may think 'eternal knowledge' means God knows everything at once in a timeless manner.
Correction: The passage is not about the nature of knowledge but about its attribute being uncreated. It argues that knowledge cannot be created in time because that would entail an eternal opposite.

Checkpoint
According to the first proof, what would be eternal if God's knowledge were originated?
A) God's power
B) Ignorance
C) God's will
D) Creation

Key Takeaway
God's knowledge must be eternal because if it were temporal, its opposite would be eternal, making knowledge impossible.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
If God's knowledge were not eternal but temporal, what would that imply about His ability to know your deepest secrets and future repentances? How does the eternity of His knowledge affect your sense of accountability and hope?
?
The argument uses the impossibility of an eternal opposite. In your life, what 'opposites' (like faith vs. disbelief) do you treat as if they can both be permanent, and how does this lesson challenge that assumption?
?
Picture yourself standing before God knowing that His knowledge of your deeds is uncreated and never-ending. Does that reality make you more careful or more grateful? Commit to one change in how you approach a hidden sin today.

Proof from Infinite Regress: God's Knowledge Must Be Eternal
Second argument for eternity of knowledge using infinite regress.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the second kalam proof that God's knowledge is eternal, based on the impossibility of infinite regress in intentions.

Core Idea
Originating knowledge would require prior knowledge, leading to an infinite chain, which is impossible.

The author presents a second argument, this time focusing on the act of originating knowledge itself. If knowledge were created, the act of creating it would require prior knowledge of that act, and that prior knowledge would also need to be created, and so on. This leads to an absurd infinite regress. Let's see how this forces us to accept that knowledge is eternal.

Guided Path
1
Follow the conditional
Read the argument: if knowledge is originated, then originating it requires intention, which is based on knowledge of that intended act. So there must be prior knowledge.
2
Recognize the regress
That prior knowledge, if also originated, would require another prior knowledge, and so on. This chain cannot go on infinitely (tasalsul is impossible).
Infinite regress is a standard kalam argument.
3
Conclude eternity
Therefore, the original knowledge cannot be originated; it must be eternal.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله : ( بعلم قديم ؛ لما سبق في القدرة ) يعني : أنَّ كونَ العالِمِ عالماً مِنْ غيرِ علم يقوم به لا يُعقل ، والدليل على كون ذلك العلم قديماً : أنَّه لو كانَ حادثاً لكانَ ضدُّهُ مِنَ الجهل ونحوه قديماً ، والقديم لا ينعدم ، فيلزمُ ألا يتّصف بالعلم أبداً ، ومصنوعاته تشهد بكذب ذلك وأيضاً : فلو كانَ العلم حادثاً لاحتاجَ في إحداثِهِ إلى علم آخر يتعلَّقُ بهِ ؛ إذ القصد إلى إحداثِهِ فرعُ العلم به ، ثم ننقل الكلام إلى العلم الآخرِ ، فيحتاجُ هو أيضاً في إحداثِهِ إلى علم آخر ، ويلزمُ التسلسل
His saying: 'With eternal knowledge, as was stated previously concerning power' means that the fact that the knower is knowing without knowledge subsisting in him is not rationally conceivable. And the proof that this knowledge is eternal is that if it were originated (hadith), then its opposite, ignorance and the like, would be eternal, and the eternal does not cease to exist, so it would necessitate that He would never be described with knowledge, and His creations testify to the falsehood of that. Also: if knowledge were originated, then in originating it, it would need another knowledge pertaining to it, because the intention to originate it is a branch of knowledge of it. Then we would transfer the discussion to the other knowledge, and it too would need another knowledge in its origination, and an infinite regress (tasalsul) would follow.
Source pages p. 303

Commentary
This passage is a commentary on a line from Sanusi's text that states God has 'eternal knowledge'. The author first explains the meaning: it is inconceivable for a knower to be knowing without an attribute of knowledge subsisting in him. Then he gives two proofs for the eternity of God's knowledge.
First proof: If God's knowledge were originated (created in time), then its opposite—ignorance—would have been eternal (because before knowledge existed, the absence of knowledge would be ignorance, and that absence would be eternal). The eternal cannot cease to exist, so God would never acquire knowledge, but creation shows that He knows.
Second proof: If knowledge were originated, the act of originating it would require prior knowledge of the intended act (since intention is based on knowledge). That prior knowledge would then need to be originated, requiring another knowledge, leading to an infinite chain (tasalsul), which is impossible. Therefore, God's knowledge must be eternal.

Key Terms
حادث
قصد
تسلسل

Checkpoint
According to the second proof, why can't knowledge be originated?
A) Because it would require an infinite number of intentions
B) Because God cannot intend
C) Because knowledge is simple
D) Because ignorance would be eternal

Key Takeaway
God's knowledge is eternal because originating it would require an impossible infinite chain of prior knowledge.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

?
The argument shows that originating knowledge requires prior knowledge. In your own life, where have you acted without first intending? How does this chain of intention remind you that every good deed must be rooted in knowledge of God?
?
This proof demands that infinite regress is impossible. Yet, we often live as if our excuses are infinite. Which excuse have you been using that, if traced back, reveals a lack of reliance on God's eternal knowledge?

Why God's Knowledge Is Not Daruri
Explaining two meanings of 'daruri' and the prohibition of applying it to God.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the term 'daruri' cannot be applied to God's knowledge, even in its non-compulsive meaning of self-evident.

Core Idea
Even a harmless meaning of 'daruri' is forbidden due to connotations of compulsion.

The author begins by defining two meanings of 'daruri'—one involving harm and need, the other meaning obtained without reflection. While the second meaning is true of God's knowledge, he explains that using the word is prohibited by Islamic law because it implies compulsion, which is inapplicable to Allah. This lesson unpacks that nuanced argument.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage on daruri
Read the Arabic passage that discusses the two meanings of 'daruri' and the ruling on applying it to God. Notice the author's precise wording.
The passage distinguishes between meaning and usage.
2
Identify the two meanings
Find where the author defines the first meaning (accompanied by harm or need) and the second (obtained without reflection). Understand why the first is impossible for God by consensus.
The second meaning is technically true but still problematic.
3
Grasp the prohibition
The author says using the word 'daruri' even for the second meaning is not permissible because of the word's connotations. Understand the principle: we avoid words that imply imperfection, even if the intended meaning is sound.
This shows the sensitivity of divine language.
4
Reflect on the summary
The author concludes that the word is either prohibited in both word and meaning (first meaning) or in word only (second meaning). Appreciate the careful distinction.

لا يوصف علمه تعالى بضرورة أو نظر ] قوله : ( مُتنزّه عن الضرورة والنظرِ ) هذا مخفوضُ نعتاً لقولهِ ) بعلم قديم ) ، والعلمُ الضروري في الأصل : هو الذي يقارنه ضرر أو حاجة (۱) ؛ كعلمنا بألمنا وجوعِنا ، ولا شك أنه بهذا المعنى مستحيل في حقِّهِ تعالى ؛ لاستحالة الضرر عليه والحاجة إجماعاً وقد يُطلَقُ الضروري على ما يحصلُ بغير نظر ، وهو بهذا المعنى صحيح في علمِهِ تعالى ، إلا أنَّ إطلاق لفظ ( الضروري ) على علمِهِ تعالى ولو بهذا المعنى.. لا يجوز شرعاً ؛ لما أفهمهُ اللفظُ مِنَ الضرورة والإلجاء . وبالجملة : فإطلاقُ لفظ ( الضروريّ ( على علمِهِ تعالى ممتنع إمَّا لفظاً ومعنى إنْ أُريد المعنى الأول ، أو لفظاً لا إِنْ أُريدَ معنی المعنى الثاني (۲) وأما استحالة كونِ علمِهِ تعالى نظريّاً فظاهر ؛ لأنَّه لو كان كذلكَ لكانَ حادثاً ؛ لما تقرَّرَ أنَّ النظرَ يُضادُّ العلم ، فالعلم النظري إنَّما يحصل بعد انصرام النظرِ ولا يجتمعُ معَهُ ، وكونُ علمهِ تعالى حادثاً محال ؛ لما عرفت قريباً مِنْ وجوب قدمه . وبهذا يظهرُ لكَ أنَّ قولَه : ( مُتنزه عن الضرورة والنظر ، وإلا قارنه
[The section: His knowledge, exalted, is not described as necessary (daruri) or acquired (nazari)] His saying: 'Free from necessity (darura) and acquiredness (nazari)' – this is in the genitive as an adjective for 'with eternal knowledge'. And necessary knowledge in origin is that which is accompanied by harm or need; like our knowledge of our pain and hunger. There is no doubt that in this meaning it is impossible with respect to Him, exalted, because of the impossibility of harm and need for Him by consensus. And sometimes 'daruri' is applied to what is obtained without reflection (nazar). In this meaning it is correct for His knowledge, exalted, except that applying the word 'daruri' to His knowledge, exalted, even in this meaning, is not permissible legally (shar'an) because of what the word conveys of compulsion and coercion. In summary: applying the word 'daruri' to His knowledge, exalted, is prevented either in word and meaning if the first meaning is intended, or in word only if the second meaning is intended. As for the impossibility of His knowledge, exalted, being nazari, it is evident, because if it were so, it would be originated (hadith), since it is established that reflection (nazar) is opposed to knowledge, so theoretical knowledge is only obtained after the cessation of reflection and does not coexist with it. And His knowledge being originated is impossible, as you have recently known from the necessity of its eternity. And by this it becomes clear to you that his saying: 'Free from necessity and reflection, otherwise it would be accompanied by...'
Source pages p. 304

Commentary
This passage is a commentary on a phrase from Sanusi's text describing God's knowledge as 'free from necessity (darura) and acquiredness (nazar)'. The author first explains the adjective agreement, then distinguishes two meanings of 'daruri': (1) knowledge accompanied by harm or need (like feeling pain or hunger), which is impossible for God by consensus; (2) knowledge obtained without reflection (i.e., intuitive). Though the second meaning is true of God's knowledge, using the word 'daruri' is forbidden in Islamic law because it implies compulsion and need, even if the intended meaning is harmless. So the word is either entirely prohibited (if meaning 1) or prohibited in usage but not in meaning (if meaning 2). Next, the author argues that God's knowledge cannot be 'nazari' (acquired through reflection) because reflection and knowledge are opposites; theoretical knowledge occurs after reflection ends, whereas God's knowledge is eternal and not subject to temporal processes. Since God's knowledge must be eternal (as proven earlier), it cannot be acquired. Thus, the description 'free from darura and nazar' is justified.

Correction: The author shows that 'daruri' in its primary sense involves harm/need, which is impossible for God. Even the secondary sense (self-evident) is not to be used because the word conveys compulsion.

Key Takeaway
We must avoid words that imply imperfection about Allah, even if the intended meaning is sound, because language carries connotations.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

This lesson shows that even a *true* meaning about Allah must be expressed in permissible language. Where in your own speech about Allah do you prioritize convenience over precision? Will you commit to examining every term you use in supplication and praise?
?
If the word 'daruri' is forbidden solely because it *implies* compulsion, how many other words in your vocabulary about Allah might carry hidden pitfalls? How can you safeguard your tongue from describing the Almighty with even an unintentional flaw?

Why God's Knowledge Is Not Nazari
Arguing against describing God's knowledge as acquired (nazari) due to temporality.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why God's knowledge cannot be described as 'nazari' (acquired through reflection).

Core Idea
Acquired knowledge requires temporality, contradicting God's eternal nature.

The author now turns to the second term: 'nazari' (acquired through reflection). He argues that if God's knowledge were nazari, it would have to be originated (hadith), because reflection and knowledge cannot coexist—knowledge comes after reflection ends. Since God's knowledge is eternal, it cannot be acquired. This lesson explains that clear proof.

Guided Path
1
Read the nazari passage
Read the Arabic passage that discusses why God's knowledge cannot be nazari. Note the logical structure: if nazari, then temporal; but temporal is impossible.
The argument is concise but powerful.
2
Understand the opposition
The author says 'النظر يُضادُّ العلم' – reflection is opposed to knowledge. They cannot coexist. Therefore, theoretical knowledge only arises after reflection ceases.
This is a key principle in epistemology.
3
Connect to eternity
Since God's knowledge is eternal (as proven earlier), it cannot be the result of a temporal process like reflection. Hence it cannot be nazari.
The conclusion is drawn from the premise of God's eternity.

Why God's Knowledge Cannot Be Described as Necessary or Acquired
Sanusi explains the prohibition of describing God's knowledge as necessary or acquired, and its universal scope.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why classical theologians prohibit describing God's knowledge as 'necessary' (ḍarūrī) or 'acquired' (naẓarī), and how His knowledge encompasses all rational judgments.

Core Idea
God's knowledge is eternal, not forced or reasoned.

In the previous lesson, we affirmed that God knows through an eternal attribute. But can we call that knowledge 'necessary' or 'acquired'? Sanusi explains that these terms, while used for human knowledge, cannot be applied to God — either because of their meaning (harm/need) or because of verbal implications (compulsion). He also clarifies that acquired knowledge would imply origination, which is impossible for God. Let's follow his reasoning step by step.

Guided Path
1
Understand the two senses of 'necessary'
Read the first part of the passage where Sanusi distinguishes between necessary knowledge in its original sense (accompanying harm/need) and a secondary sense (knowledge without reasoning). Note that the first sense is impossible for God by consensus.
2
See why even the second sense is disallowed
Even though God's knowledge is not acquired through reasoning, applying the term 'necessary' to God is forbidden because the word implies compulsion (ḍarūra). The prohibition is verbal, not conceptual.
Sanusi says: 'The speaker's primary intent is about meanings, not words.'
3
Examine why God's knowledge cannot be acquired
Sanusi argues that acquired knowledge is originated because it occurs after reasoning ceases. Since God's knowledge is eternal, it cannot be acquired. This is straightforward.
He refers to an earlier proof of God's knowledge being eternal.
4
Recognize the rhetorical device and scope of knowledge
Notice how the matn's phrase is an example of orderly laf wa nashr. Also, understand that God's knowledge covers all rational categories: necessary, possible, impossible.
This shows the precision and elegance of kalam argumentation.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
لا يوصف علمه تعالى بضرورة أو نظر ] قوله : ( مُتنزّه عن الضرورة والنظرِ ) هذا مخفوضُ نعتاً لقولهِ ) بعلم قديم ) ، والعلمُ الضروري في الأصل : هو الذي يقارنه ضرر أو حاجة ؛ كعلمنا بألمنا وجوعِنا ، ولا شك أنه بهذا المعنى مستحيل في حقِّهِ تعالى ؛ لاستحالة الضرر عليه والحاجة إجماعاً وقد يُطلَقُ الضروري على ما يحصلُ بغير نظر ، وهو بهذا المعنى صحيح في علمِهِ تعالى ، إلا أنَّ إطلاق لفظ ( الضروري ) على علمِهِ تعالى ولو بهذا المعنى.. لا يجوز شرعاً ؛ لما أفهمهُ اللفظُ مِنَ الضرورة والإلجاء . وبالجملة : فإطلاقُ لفظ ( الضروريّ ) على علمِهِ تعالى ممتنع إمَّا لفظاً ومعنى إنْ أُريد المعنى الأول ، أو لفظاً لا إِنْ أُريدَ معنى المعنى الثاني وأما استحالة كونِ علمِهِ تعالى نظريّاً فظاهر ؛ لأنَّه لو كان كذلكَ لكانَ حادثاً ؛ لما تقرَّرَ أنَّ النظرَ يُضادُّ العلم ، فالعلم النظري إنَّما يحصل بعد انصرام النظرِ ولا يجتمعُ معَهُ ، وكونُ علمهِ تعالى حادثاً محال ؛ لما عرفت قريباً مِنْ وجوب قدمه . ووبهذا يظهرُ لكَ أنَّ قولَه : ( مُتنزه عن الضرورة والنظر ، وإلا قارنه الضرر ، أو كانَ حادثاً ) مِنْ بابِ اللف والنشر المرتَّبِ ؛ فاللَّفُ قوله : ( الضرورة والنظرِ ) ، والنشرُ في قوله : ( قارنه الضرر ، أو كان حادثاً ) ، فمقارنة الضررِ دليلُ استحالة كونِ علمِهِ تعالى ضروريّاً ، والحدوث دليلُ استحالة كونِهِ نظريّاً ، واقتصرتُ في الضروري على المعنى الأول والأصلي فيه ؛ لأنَّ منعه معنوي بخلافِ الثاني ؛ فإِنَّ منعه لفظي ، وكلام المتكلّم بالقصدِ الأولِ إنَّما هو في المعاني ، لا في الألفاظ . قوله : ( ويتعلَّق بجميع أقسام الحكم العقلي ) يعني : يتعلَّق علمه تعالى بكل واجب وكل جائز وكلُّ مستحيل ، فلا نهاية لمعلوماتِه جلَّ وعزّ ، واستدلاله على هذا المطلب ظاهر ، وبالله التوفيق .
His knowledge, exalted be He, is not described as necessary or acquired. His statement: 'Free from necessity and reasoning' is in the genitive as an adjective for His statement 'with eternal knowledge.' Necessary knowledge originally is that which accompanies harm or need, like our knowledge of our pain and hunger, and without doubt it is in this sense impossible with respect to Him, exalted be He, because of the impossibility of harm and need for Him by consensus. And necessary is sometimes applied to what is obtained without reasoning, and in this sense it is valid for His knowledge, exalted be He, except that the application of the term 'necessary' to His knowledge, exalted be He, even in this sense, is not permissible legally because the word implies compulsion and coercion. In summary: the application of the term 'necessary' to His knowledge, exalted be He, is prohibited either in wording and meaning if the first meaning is intended, or in wording only if the second meaning is intended. As for the impossibility of His knowledge, exalted be He, being acquired, it is obvious, because if it were so, it would be originated, since it is established that reasoning is opposed to knowledge; for acquired knowledge only occurs after the cessation of reasoning and does not coexist with it, and His knowledge, exalted be He, being originated is impossible due to what you recently learned about the necessity of its eternity. And by this it becomes clear to you that his statement: 'Free from necessity and reasoning, otherwise harm would accompany it, or it would be originated' is an example of orderly laf wa nashr (weaving and unweaving); the laf (weaving) is his statement 'necessity and reasoning,' and the nashr (unweaving) is in his statement 'harm would accompany it, or it would be originated.' Thus, the accompaniment of harm is evidence of the impossibility of His knowledge being necessary, and origination is evidence of the impossibility of it being acquired. And I have confined the term 'necessary' to the first and original meaning because its prohibition is semantic, unlike the second; for its prohibition is verbal, and the speaker's primary intent is about meanings, not words. His statement: 'And it relates to all categories of rational judgment' means: His knowledge, exalted be He, relates to every obligatory, every possible, and every impossible, so there is no limit to His information, glorified and exalted is He, and his proof of this point is evident, and with Allah is success.
Source pages p. 304

Commentary
This passage is a commentary on a matn line stating that God's knowledge is free from necessity (ḍarūrī) and acquired (naẓarī). The author explains why these terms cannot apply to God. First, he clarifies the term 'necessary knowledge' (al-ʿilm al-ḍarūrī) which originally means knowledge accompanied by harm or need, such as knowledge of one's own pain or hunger. This sense is obviously impossible for God, as He is free from harm and need by consensus. However, 'necessary' is sometimes used in a second sense: knowledge obtained without reasoning (bi-ghayr naẓar). In this sense, it might seem applicable because God's knowledge is not acquired through reasoning. Yet, even in this second sense, applying the term 'necessary' to God's knowledge is not permissible legally (sharʿan) because the word itself implies compulsion and coercion, which are unfitting for God. Thus, the prohibition is either in both wording and meaning (if the first sense is intended) or in wording alone (if the second sense is intended). Next, the author argues that God's knowledge cannot be acquired (naẓarī) because acquired knowledge is originated (ḥādith): reasoning (naẓar) is opposite to knowledge, and acquired knowledge only occurs after reasoning ceases and does not coexist with it. Since God's knowledge is eternal (qadīm) as previously established, it cannot be originated and hence cannot be acquired. The author then points out that the matn's phrase 'otherwise harm would accompany it, or it would be originated' is an example of the rhetorical device 'laf wa nashr' (weaving and unweaving) in an orderly arrangement. The 'laf' (weaving) is the mention of 'necessity and reasoning,' and the 'nashr' (unweaving) is the mention of 'harm would accompany it' (for necessity) and 'or it would be originated' (for reasoning). Thus, the accompaniment of harm proves the impossibility of God's knowledge being necessary, and origination proves the impossibility of it being acquired. The author notes that he focused on the first meaning of 'necessary' because its prohibition is based on meaning, while the second meaning's prohibition is merely verbal, and the primary concern of the speaker is with meanings, not words. Finally, the passage concludes with another matn phrase: 'And it relates to all categories of rational judgment,' meaning God's knowledge encompasses every obligatory (wājib), every possible (jāʾiz), and every impossible (mustaḥīl), so His information is infinite. The author states that the proof of this is evident, and ends with 'bi-llāh al-tawfīq.'

Key Terms
ضروري
نظري
علم قديم
لف ونشر
الحكم العقلي

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think that describing God's knowledge as 'necessary' in the sense of 'without reasoning' is acceptable.
Correction: Sanusi clarifies that even that sense is prohibited because the word 'necessary' (ḍarūrī) itself implies compulsion (ḍarūra), which is unfitting for God. The prohibition is verbal, not conceptual.

Checkpoint
According to Sanusi, why is it impossible for God's knowledge to be acquired (naẓarī)?
A) Because acquired knowledge is always accompanied by harm.
B) Because acquired knowledge is originated (ḥādith).
C) Because acquired knowledge is obtained without reasoning.
D) Because acquired knowledge is limited in scope.

Key Takeaway
God's knowledge is eternal and all-encompassing, and although it is not acquired through reasoning, it is also not called 'necessary' because the term carries inappropriate connotations of compulsion.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you call upon Allah or describe His knowledge in your thoughts, do you ever unconsciously imagine Him as having to 'figure things out' or as knowing things because they are 'obvious'? What does this lesson reveal about the subtle disrespect in such assumptions?
?
If Allah's knowledge cannot be called 'necessary' because that word implies compulsion, what does that tell you about His absolute freedom? Is there an area of your life where you have been acting as if Allah is constrained—by your expectations, by your circumstances? Will you repent from that today?
?
This lesson teaches that Allah's knowledge covers everything, including what is impossible. Think of something you consider 'impossible' for yourself—a change, a forgiveness, a provision. Does your heart truly believe that Allah knows that impossibility intimately? How would your dua change if you prayed believing He already knows the impossible about you?
